0307593495|2.0|As a fan of the Stieg Larsson trilogy, it's probably only natural that I was spurred to branch out to other Scandinavian writers.  My first experience with Mankell was with the novel \\"The Man from Beijing\\".  I really enjoyed that novel and, having heard good things about the Wallander series, I was anxious to give it a shot as well.  Perhaps it's due to my coming into the series so late in the game, but I was very disappointed.<br /><br />The mystery itself is a decent one--that is, when the book is actually dealing with the mystery.  Though I did sometimes find it difficult to follow because I'm entirely unfamiliar with the events occurring in Sweden during the Cold War, the mystery itself was a pretty good tale of spies and intrigue.  The problem was that the narrative meandered so much that, by the time I reached the climax of the novel, I was pretty worn out and eager to finish.  The end result of this was that the ending didn't have the impact it probably should have had.  This lack of impact was probably also due to my inability connect with Hakan and Louise, the main characters involved.  Louise isn't very well developed, and the reader learns little about Hakan outside of the context of his mysterious disappearance.<br /><br />However, the biggest failure of the book is its plodding pacing.  Any excitement generated by the mystery plot is negated by page after page of useless detail.  While I appreciate a good literary mystery, I just found this one so cumbersome.  I think a good third of the book could have been edited out and it wouldn't have hurt the main narrative in any way.  At times, the extraneous detail is so egregious.  To provide a specific example, it seemed like every time Wallander called either Ytterberg or Nordlander, they would tell him they'd have to call him back and then, several unimportant sentences later, they would.  Why does the reader even need this detail in the first place?  There are many examples like this throughout the book.  While I do appreciate a densely detailed, layered narrative, it really only works if the pieces are all tied to advancing the plot, which is not the case here.<br /><br />I also found that I just couldn't warm to Wallander.  As I've said, this is my first book, so I can't speak to what this character is like in other novels.  In this one, though, he is so morose and so often wallowing in self-pitying thoughts of his own mortality that I just found myself disliking him.  He was such a downer I didn't want to spend much time with him.  This dual narrative structure just didn't work for me.  The book should either have been a mystery, in which case the bulk of Wallander's personal woes should have been cut out, or it should have been a book about a man struggling with the idea of nearing the end of his life, in which case the mystery should have been cut out.<br /><br />Given this, it's very disappointing that, when the end comes about, it's told in such a hurried fashion.  After reading pages and pages about what Wallander eats, how many naps he takes, etc., it's very jarring to suddenly have the ending slapped together in the last thirty pages or so.  It felt to me like Mankell was just tired at this point.  Since he's stated that this is his last Wallander novel, I don't think I'm giving anything away by saying that I had a distinct impression that he wanted to firmly shut the door on the character, and it shows in his epilogue.  I found that section particularly depressing and not very well-written.  Even though I don't know much about Wallander, I really felt like he probably deserved better, seeing as how he's been the subject of an entire series of novels.<br /><br />I've given this book two stars instead of one, though it's probably more of a one and a half star novel.  Because I did feel that the mystery was decent and would have been a lot more compelling had it been better written, I couldn't disregard this one entirely.  However, I was extremely unimpressedby it and am very unlikely to go back and read the previous Wallander novels.e previous Wallander novels.

I truly agreed with most of what he said about Big Pharma, and even that pillar of alternative medicine \\"antioxidants.\\" But here is one problem that I think exemplies the flawed manner of critique that is used at various points in the book. As an example of Mr. Goodacre's flawed methodology, in his chapter entitled \\"The Nonsense du Jour\\" he cites a particular nutritionist who claims that Tumeric (Curcumin) \\"has the potential to prevent cancers, especially prostate cancer.\\" Mr Goldacre's critique of the claim aims at the fact that Curcumin is metabolized in the liver and for this reason is not bioavailable...useless - thus rendering the nutrient and the claim to be \\"Nonsense (du Jour).\\"  YES, this is a known fact about Curcumin. However, over the past few years far more bioavailable forms of Curcumin have become available, one, BCM-95, increased the absorption nearly 7-fold (check the studies). There have been NUMEROUS positive in vivo and in vitro studies documenting the role of Curcumin in a great variety of maladies, including cancer. Not a few quacks, but real studies...good studies. Why does MR. G not mention these?  Mr Goodacre argues that many of these studies are rodent or in vitro and might have very different effects in the human body. But many newer studies are being compiled in humans. About 5000 positive studies have been performed on this substance. Yes, most are in-vitro, or on rats, but there are many in-vivo studies, mostly involving suppression of NF kappa-B.  The point is that Mr Goodacre's attacks are sometimes one dimensional.  If I foster an attack on religion by exclusively attacking fundamentalists, I have done a disservice to the greater discipline. In the same way Mr G. picks one or two or even three individuals, demonstrates their flawed methodologies, and believes he reveals a pervasive ignorance inherent in the entire discipline, say \\"nutritionists\\", or \\"Vitamins, or \\"Holistic\\".   For the most part Mr. Goodacre's attack is on the dumbed-down, media driven \\"Reader's Digest\\" approach to what's \\"out there\\". I could not agree more. But for some of us that look a bit deeper, we parse the nonsense from authentic gold (nearly exclusively through the examination of studies). He would say that \\"those not in the know\\" might be misled by nutritionist hucksters. Good point. However, by the same token \\"those not in the know\\"  might be misled by Mr Goodacre, for example, into thinking that nutritionists are clueless, and nutrients (as pills) are worthless. But here, he has selected examples that only tend to affirm his objections, conveniently leaving out the other side.  -\\"disqualification by design.\\" That is my main objection. Considering the many THOUSANDS of studies demonstrating the efficacy of certain over the counter nutrients for example, including Curcumin, and more valuable versions of \\"Holistic\\" (an \\"invented\\" field  or term he also bashes), I question Mr Goodacre's methodology as writer of science.  If you have read the book and want a demonstration of how shortsighted MR. G is with respect to the above example (I only chose one) go to the \\"Google Scholar\\" browser and type in \\"Curcumin.\\" After perusing that ask yourself \\"Is Mr. Goodacre being honest or simply self serving?\\" OR \\"Are the 5-star reviewers above who believe Mr. Goodacre has enlightened us, as uninformed as those who simply believe a product will prevent Cancer because some guy on HSN says it will?\\"

0517548232|4.0|Be advised:  Mr. Hazlitt has a deep admiration for Fr√©d√©ric Bastiat and approaches economics in much the same way.  That his book is a heavy dose of free market economics thus should be no surprise.  Neither should be his approach:  that of judging economic policies in view of their total effects, not just on a favored interest group, but on all groups, and not just for the short-term, but for the long-term as well.  For over sixty years this is the tune Hazlitt has been singing.  And surprisingly, it is a tune that still strikes a modern-day chord.   Deficit spending, inflation, farm subsidies, trade tariffs--pick your modern day issue and you'll find a chapter or two directed to it here.  Looking at each through what some might call an Austrian lens, Hazlitt focuses on their supposed fallacies in an upfront, un-intimidating manner.  It makes for an enticing presentation--which is likely the reason for its stunning decades-long resilience--but admittedly, its age is beginning to show.    The intricacies of modern finance are not to be untangled here, nor is the role of finance in modern economics even acknowledged.  Similarly, though the importance of trade is trumpeted, the complexities of globalization are not to be forecast. For these issues, you'll have to turn to books like Greg Ip's [[ASIN:0470621664 The Little Book of Economics: How the Economy Works in the Real World (Little Books. Big Profits)]] or Alan Greenspan's surprisingly readable [[ASIN:0143114166 The Age of Turbulence: Adventures in a New World]] (Tom Friedman's [[ASIN:0312425074 The World Is Flat 3.0: A Brief History of the Twenty-first Century]] also isn't bad, but it is a bit too folksy).  Approaching economics from an initial condition of equilibrium also seems a bit flat, if only because it describes an economy that doesn't exist.    Hazlitt's prescience regarding the results of unnaturally low interest rates, deficit spending, and government subsidies of the housing market, however, are astonishingly accurate in view of the recent economic malaise.  That government subsidized mortgages with little to no downpayment from buyers could lead to a glut of bad loans, an oversupply of housing, and a cancerous malinvestment of capital are lessons we are ruing today and risks he warned of decades ago. That we didn't recognize them sooner supports Mr. Hazlitt's ultimate conclusion that our problems are less economic than political, and, with the rest, makes his short little book on economics exceptionally enticing, if not surprisingly resilient.

0393064476|4.0|The Swerve, by Stephen Greenblatt a professor at Harvard University, is about how Poggio Bracciolini, a temporarily unemployed Vatican bureaucrat, found a copy of the ancient poem by Lucretius entitled On the Nature of Things, and the subsequent influence of this poem.  Lucretius was an Epicurean, an ancient philosophy that held that all existence came down to the combinations and separations of atoms. Apparently he wrote this up in very good Latin, which was part of the attraction to Poggio and other readers. Greenblatt's title is a reference to how Lucretius referred to unexpected, unpredicted combinations of matter. Poggio seems to have found the originally manuscript in a monastery in southern Germany, though which monastery is unknown and the manuscript does not appear to have survived.  The copy he had made was the one from which others were made during the Renissiance.  Many years later 3 manuscript copies from the 9th century AD turned up.   Although not precisely atheistic, as Lucretius did not deny gods exist, the poem indicates that they are not interested in us,  and therefore worshipping them is beside the point.  Lucretius denied the existence of an afterlife.  As one might imagine, this doctrine was not shared by the Catholic Church, although matters did not become really sticky until the Council of Trent in 1551, during which it become doctrine that there was a distinction between the appearance (the taste, smell, sight and other characteristics) of the sacrament and its substance.  As an atomic theory would not seem to allow such a distinction to be made, the ideas of Lucretius were not in good standing.  Still, the poem does not seem to have been officially prohibited by the Catholic Church.  In any case, it was too late to destroy the poem after printing had been invented. The poem seems to have influenced Giordano Bruno, Galileo Galilei, and Montaigne, as well as disturbing Thomas More.  The idea of atoms became a commonplace among intellectuals.I first posted a very similar review on goodreads.com

0375826742|3.0|Brisingr is the third installment in the Inheritance Cycle, preceded by Eldest. In book three, Eragon and his dragon Saphira are at the peak of their training and are almost ready to face Galbatorix, the evil ruler of Alagaesia.<br /><br />There are a few points I want to stress about this book. The first would be that I don't think Christopher Paolini needed to change the Inheritance Trilogy into the Inheritance Cycle. When I first saw the book at Barnes and Noble, I thought, \\"Wow, if he can make the book that huge, then it's going to be an awesome climax of the trilogy!\\" I was wrong. I read it...and read it...( took me over 7 hours), and by the time I was three fourths of the way through, needless to say, I was a little disappointed. The Ra'zac were killed off in the first couple chapters, which, yes, it was inevitable, but I wish he drew it out a bit (I LOVED the Ra'zac), and that leaves Eragon with plenty of time to complete his training, decide whether he's going to continue chasing Arya or not, and engage in a final duel with Galbatorix. But instead, as pointed out by numerous other reviews, we get another 600 pages of training, more stuff about the Varden and Nasuada, a bit of funny-sounding dialogue, and endless descriptions of Ellesmera and Du Weldenvarden. The 200-page section about dwarf politics and leaders really left me groaning and thinking, \\"Why doesn't Murtagh just come in with Thorn and torch all these stupid Dwarves?\\"<br /><br />The second point is the development of Eragon's character. He's definitely come a long way since book one, and has grown into a smarter, more skilled elf/human hybrid. I particularly liked him in this book more than in Eldest, where, to put it quite simply, the chapters with Eragon and Oromis bored me to death. His sword skills are fantastic and he uses magic much more wisely than simply yelling, \\"Brisingr!\\" and launching an arrow at Urgals. But he's not the fun, straightforward farm boy he used to be. \\"Eragon\\" is my favorite book of the series, and I especially liked his character in that book. In Brisingr, he's kind of isolated and doesn't make a good scene around other characters. He's never really happy or really sad: he seems to be in a perpetual stoic mood throughout the entire book It's like he's always thinking, \\"You can be friends with me, but I'm a Dragon Rider, so show me some respect.\\"<br /><br />Third point: the book is pretty graphic. I'm a fifteen-year-old guy, and I'm not afraid to admit that I do enjoy a lot of blood and gore in violent scenes, but even I thought some of it was a little over the top and wouldn't really encourage my eleven-year-old brother to read it. There are endless sentences describing Roran's hammer \\"smashing\\", \\"crunching\\", or \\"bashing\\" down on various parts of his enemy's bodies, blood \\"spraying\\" through the air and bone being crushed...I think you get the idea. I even kind of cringed at the part where a soldier pulled an arrow out of his foot, and \\"chunks of meat clung to the arrowhead.\\"  I mean, sure, fantasy offers more opportunities for gore, with the various forms of magic, weapons, and mythical beasts, but I think it should be kept on a realistic level, not blown out of proportion. I'm not saying Paolini went completely overboard with his descriptions, but he could have toned it down just a tad. I'd be willing to bet that he'd get more readers if he wasn't so enthusiastic about graphic violence. If your kids are under twelve and want to read it, I'm not telling you don't let them, but I don't strongly encourage it.<br /><br />Point 4- WHERE'S MURTAGH? He's my absolute favorite, but I think you see him the most in Book 1! He's in Brisingr (I think, it's been a while since I read it), but not as much as I would like. If there were more chapters of him using his magic and dueling Eragon, I would be pretty happy. When I was reading Eldest and he appears at the end as a Dragon Rider, I was really excited, but then he disappears again and I wasthinking, \\"What? That's it? Come on.\\"<br /><br />Point 5- The chapters with Roran were engaging, but when he gets into close action, Christopher Paolini seems to have a little too much fun with the fact that he is protected by wards cast on him by Eragon, because he's this invincible, terrifying guy who kills anyone he lays eyes on. One thing I thought was pretty cheesy was when they were sacking a village and Roran is attacked, he kills almost two hundred- 200!- men by himself, with only a hammer and shield. At the end of the battle, it says there was a \\"mountain\\" of corpses that Roran created, and all he's got to show for it is a bunch of scratches, some cracked ribs, and a crossbow bolt in his shoulder that he doesn't seem to even care about.<br /><br />So, overall, I guess I wouldn't say I don't like this book because I did enjoy reading it, but I don't think it really needed to be split into two books. All the vital stuff for the Inheritance Cycle could have fit in 750 pages. But I have to say, the cliffhanger ending did have me hooked, and I'm waiting as patiently as I can for Book 4. Keep it up, CP!
B004LWZW42|4.0|Origin films are pretty popular among the superhero alumni.  Every rabid fan of their favorite spandex-clad heroes wants to see their story done justice on the big screen, and an origin story is the preferable route considering that their humble beginnings are always to most interesting way to instill a renewed adoration for that said hero.<br /><br />`X-Men: First Class' is one of the latest in this genre (one of many to come out this year, which was basically superhero madness over the summer) and it works to a degree thanks to the acting that anchors the film's core.<br /><br />The film tells of the beginning of the X-Men, or more specifically, the beginning of Professor X and Magneto's rivalry.  As you probably already know, they started off friends and became enemies (but civil enemies) thanks to a conflict of interests and drastically differing attitudes towards cohabitation with humans (Magneto wants them dead, X wants to be their friends).  Here, we see a similar opening to the one witnessed in Bryan Singer's 2000 film, with Magneto (then known as Erik Lehnsherr) exhibiting his mutant abilities in a fit of rage after being separated from his mother in Nazi Germany.  We then get a small glimpse into the very different lives of Charles Xavier and Erik as one has a very posh and privileged upbringing and the other is subjected to experimental horrors because of his gifts.  Now, as adults, they come together in an effort to stop the evil Sebastian Shaw (who has a deeper connection to Erik) from destroying mankind.  To do this they gather a group of mutant teens who are eager to harness their abilities for the betterment of mankind.<br /><br />As a whole, this works.  The graphics are pretty amazing (those chains in the water were effectively eerie, and the whole Banshee water scene was fantastic) and the actors all pull out great performances, especially your two leads.  McAvoy and Fassbender are both exciting young leads.  They have already proven themselves as terrific Oscar caliber actors (watch `Atonement' or `Fish Tank' for proof of that) and they bring that polished actorly bravado to their respective roles here.  McAvoy flirts his way through the opening with charisma and charm and then effortlessly slips into a more mature take of his character, but it's Fassbender's chilling unveiling of Erik's intentions that really catch your breath.<br /><br />The main issue I have with this film is the first half.  It's sloppy.  Because this is essentially THREE origin stories (that of Xavier, Erik and the X-Men) it struggles to stay grounded while it jumps back and forth, and so it fails to build a real solid foundation for either of the main stars backstories, and it lazily slops together the actual X-Men team.  Some of the dialog and staging of the teens can be a little cheesy, but its forgivable considering that this is a superhero movie.  I just wish that they had been more focused in their storytelling, maybe shafting the actual X-Men team, which is vastly less interesting than Erik and Charles, and concentrated on building a powerful story surrounding those two comic book heavyweights.<br /><br />Alas, this is still a lot of fun, and the action never stops so that is a plus. 1451648537|5.0|In 42 chapters narrated through 573 pages, master biographer Walter Isaacson has done an extraordinary job of chronicling the life of a man that probably lived 5 lifetimes in one all too brief 56 years. Remember that Steve Jobs picked Isaacson to write this masterpiece, as early as 2004 when it was the very private head of Apple that called the author while the author was head of the famous Aspen Institute. Isaacson resisted at first. One reason was that Jobs was still alive, and in the prime of his productivity. The author thought it would be a good idea 20 years down the road. Jobs being intensely private did not share the recently received news of cancer.<br /><br />It would be 2009, five years later when Isaacson would be talking with Jobs' wife Laurene Powell and she would tell him that you really should write this book. The author would then receive a call that same year on New Year's Eve day from Jobs and once again the issue was raised. Finally, Jobs being persuasive told the author that he the writer would have complete editorial control. Jobs did not even want to read the draft when it was done. He would encourage all his associates, friends, and even those antagonistic to him to be completely candid and open with Isaacson. Ultimately the only objection Jobs would raise was to the photograph that the publisher came up with for the cover. In the end, Jobs got the cover he thought was more appropriate and who could begrudge him that.<br /><br />If the reader has read any of the author's previous works dealing with Einstein, Ben Franklin, or Henry Kissinger, you will recognize that he is methodical, thorough, and very demanding in his choice of language, and what he presents as facts. In the case of Steve Jobs he conducted 40 interviews over 2 years, plus over 100 interactions with friends, adversaries, family members, and colleagues. The book bears witness to the thoroughness of the author's technique.<br /><br />What the Book is Telling the Reader<br /><br />This is very simply the story of a boy or was abandoned by his birth parents, raised by loving and caring adoptive parents, and went on in spite of the demons that accompanied him throughout his life to change the world on his own terms in a positive fashion. What a wonderful legacy to leave the world with. He demonstrated to humanity that regardless of how you come onto this planet, you can choose to have your own impact. You do not have to accept what is. Everything originates with an internalized idea or fantasy, and then from there it is the reader's choice as to whether to process it into a reality, and Jobs created many realities.<br /><br />In every book, there is that one page, or paragraph, sometimes just a sentence or even a phrase that is MAGICAL, that makes the entire book worth reading. For some readers it will be in the title of Chapter 11, which is called The Reality Distortion Field. What Jobs has created in this tight short, but priceless phrase is that there is always reality, what truly is. In the minds however are prejudices or distortions, or sometimes delete other truths. What this means is that in the end, each reader, each inventory has their own version of reality. Although this individually created reality is not the reality that everyone shares, it is still reality to that individual person.  This is the Reality Distortion Field, and it well might be the most amazing concept in the book and could well change your understanding of how to change your own world. The reader might consider reading Chapter 11 thoroughly.<br /><br />After reading the book, the question arises whether there was anyone else like him past or present. In the last 100 years or so you have had other inventors like Thomas Edison, Nikolai Tesla, and more recently Edwin Land of Polaroid (the instant processing camera). When you read the Isaacson book, Jobs specifically mentions that Edwin Land was a childhood hero of his. What develops out of this is that even the masters have masters. It is understood that none of us do it alone. Steve Jobs shows us how to achieve something truly worthwhile, and his biography is a guidebook to the useful life. Like others who have stood On the Shoulders of Giants (OTSOG), Jobs took us to new heights as a civilization. Throughout the book is his continual embrace of the technology of the moment, combining it with art, humanity, and engineering prowess to bring about a whole new way of living and interacting while touching hundreds of millions of people and then the whole planet.<br /><br />He did something so strange, so unique by re-inventing Apple after the success of the personal computer. Once a company leads a revolution as did Apple back in the 1980's, never, ever does the same corporation lead another revolution again. This is precisely what Steve Jobs did. He led several more business revolutions. Whether it was music, or movie pictures, or Tablet, even the cell phone, if he embraced the technology, he changed it, and by changing it he changed civilization and life on the planet. Just look at the six industries that the book points out that he revolutionized:<br /><br />* Movies with PIXAR<br /><br />* Music<br /><br />* The Phone itself<br /><br />* Tablet Computing<br /><br />* Digital Publishing<br /><br />* Retail Stores<br /><br />All the time he is being driven by demons, by a past that haunts him, by a father that disavowed him, by business failures including betrayal by his handpicked CEO at Apple who then proceeds to force an unwilling Jobs out of Apple. In spite of the demons, the hardship, the failures, Jobs builds a life, an unbelievable life based on inventiveness, imagination, and sustained innovation or as he states in the book; he built my life around the intersection of science and the humanities. Many others have done this successfully in the last century, in their minds only. Only Jobs was able to bring these thoughts, dreams, and images into a physically reality with the creation of computers, phones, musical machines, and movies that revolutionized how the reader look at things, how people hear them, and feel them.<br /><br />PIXAR is a case in point. As explained by Isaacson, George Lucas owned PIXAR. A potential divorce settlement forced Lucas to sell off a great deal of his ownership it to Jobs for $10 million, $5 million of which was for capital. Disney was offered the deal first, and walked away without even taking a real look. Jobs recognized the new reality of PIXAR immediately and wrote a check buying it for what amounted to peanuts.<br /><br />Less than a decade later, Jobs would sell his ownership to Disney for billions of dollars, making perhaps 500 times on his investment to the same guys who passed the first time. This man was not motivated by money (even though he was an excellent businessman), but by creation, that is clear. The active process of invention is what moved him. In the book he says that Apple does no market research, for he creates for people what they want before they even realize what they want. They only realize it when they see it, after he has created it. How neat is that?<br /><br />CONCLUSION:<br /><br />In the very beginning of the book in the Table of Contents, Isaacson demonstrates that he has put together a very special book. The author would give a title to each chapter that would be short and to the point, but then he would add a scripted phrase. As examples:<br /><br />Chapter 1:  Childhood:  Abandoned and Chosen<br /><br />Chapter 2:  Odd Couple:  The Two Steve's<br /><br />Chapter 3:  The Dropout:  Turn On, Tune In<br /><br />The chapter titles demonstrated so much thought and brilliance, and appropriateness for the topic covered that Isaacson demonstrates that he had spent enormous amounts of time covering every base, making sure that he had his subject down pat. In reading this book, the reader learns more than about the life of an extraordinary man, for there are many extraordinary men and women. You have the story of an entire revolution, the last 30 or 40 years of Silicon Valley are covered here. What made America the tech leader of the world, the envy of the earth is covered here? Focusing, and executing, and simply getting things done is covered here.<br /><br />Perhaps more importantly than anything else, Jobs and this book is a testament to every human being's struggle to become. To fulfill the individual and unique destiny's each person has, regardless of how much time that person is allotted to fulfill them. In the case of Steve Jobs, it was much too short, but then there is still time for the rest of us. B00009Y3L4|4.0|CABARET is one of those films that entered into cinema history as soon as it premiered. I had never seen the film and had some misconceptions about the story. Only recently did I realize CABARET is a fictionalized version of historical fact. It's based on the diaries of Christopher Isherwood and his time spent in Berlin in the early 1930's. It was an exciting and frightening time. It was the last days of a decadent lifestyle that permeated Berlin as the Nazi horror was beginning to unfold.<br /><br />CABARET was a stage play loosely based on Christopher Isherwood's diaries. Bob Fosse was hired to direct the film version and his desire was to make the Kit Kat Club more of the back story as he returned more to the roots of the Isherwood's book. Here is the magic of the story as two very different personalities collide in a very uncertain time. Michael York does a remarkable job as Brian, the seemingly proper Englishman with a secret. Liza Minelli shines in her signature roll as Sally Bowles. CABARET was undoubtedly her career-defining roll as the talented but very troubled Sally. Unlike the actual Sally in Isherwood's books, this Sally can sing and has an amazing voice. Brian and Sally soon embark on a very nontraditional relationship that goes deeply into their characters.<br /><br />Joel Grey stars as the somewhat androgynous Master of Ceremonies. He has no speaking roll in the film, his songs and performances often directly address the Nazi menace. It's rare to see a film where all the main characters seem to be perfect for their roles. Only Liza Minelli could be Sally Bowles just as only Michael York could be Brian. I found the story of Brian and Sally to be fascinating and more interesting than the actual Cabaret scenes. How did fate bring two very different people together in a very difficult time.<br /><br />CABARET was released in 1972 and I was amazed that it addressed so many fairly taboo subjects of the day--homosexuality, promiscuity, abortion and racism. There is no doubt CABARET must have been somewhat shocking in 1972 with its daring honesty.<br /><br />Bob Fosse strove for realism and the movie is a real step back into 1931 Berlin. CABARET was film on location in Berlin. You can get a great sense of that time and the ominous storm clouds that were forming. It's hard to imagine CABARET with a different director or cast. It is a magical blend of talents.have been somewhat shocking in 1972 with its daring honesty.   Bob Fosse strove for realism and the movie is a real step back into 1931 Berlin. Fine, that's good, it's not dumbed down with recipes and whatnot. Despite some good advice (eat whole plant-based foods, avoid reductionism in science, industry's too cozy with government)--it became increasingly rhetorical and argumentative and I came away a little disappointed.   I first began having my doubts about this book when it seemed rather... one-sided... his argument is basically ALL animal products are bad, ALL plant products are good, and he lists study after study to prove it. Some of his reasoning is a bit confusing (he says there's \\"only\\" a 1/64 chance that there could be 5 studies showing a statistically significant link, mentioning in the same breath that there were 5 studies showing no link, for example.) I'm not familiar with this field, so I'm not sure if he's cherry picking his data or what, but the way he constantly goes back to the same conclusion and proselytizes without even saying \\"but...\\" or presenting other studies makes me think that there are other data points out there he's not presenting. Nothing is this cut and dry in science.   Some of it is downright unprofessional in a book that purports to be full of facts: for example, he spends 2-3 chapters cynically criticizing the coziness of the food industry and governmental health boards in the US, until 1) it starts to sound a bit like a conspiracy theory, 2) it's irrelevant to people wanting to get advice on health. Yes, there is a too-cozy relationship, but he insinuates that milk causes type 1 diabetes in infants and that the milk lobby suppresses this research purposely. He argues that a vegan diet can reverse type 2 diabetes, which sounds like wishful thinking to any rational person.  He name drops constantly, and tries to bludgeon people into trusting his advice because he's a research scientist, while criticizing other scientists in the same breath. At one point he claims that friends and family may criticize your habits, but it's because \\"deep down, they know their diet isn't very healthy and find it threatening that someone else is able to give up unhealthy eating habits when they cannot,\\" getting in the realm of psychological manipulation. He suggests that cold turkey is the way to go here, comparing meat products to cigarettes. How could you take someone seriously when they dot a nutrition book based on ~statistically significant scientific studies with seriously unprofessional asides like this?  I'm really disappointed I invested time going through this book when its message was so basic (tl;dr his message is \\"go vegan and don't worry about dietary imbalances because there are none\\") and most of it was reading about possibly cherry-picked studies, the china study (sample size: 64), or his suspicions about corporate america and government health corruption. Perhaps I should have just stopped when I got to the part where he claimed that you could stop type 2 diabetes with a vegan diet.

B005WTR4ZI|5.0|In \\"Race Against the Machine\\", economists Erik Brynjolfsson and Andrew McAfee ask the question: Could technology be destroying jobs? They then expand on that to explore whether advancing information technology might be an important contributor to the current unemployment disaster. The authors argue very convincingly that the answer to both questions is YES.<br /><br />The book is very readable and includes lots of links to supporting evidence (both statistical and anecdotal). The authors do a good job of focusing on how computer technology is accelerating exponentially and how computers are a \\"general purpose technology\\", in other words, a special technology that can affect just about anything else and have much bigger impact than more narrowly focused innovations.<br /><br />I thought a really good example involved automated driving. In 2005, two other economists suggested that it would be \\"hard to imagine\\" computers ever being able to handle driving in traffic. Yet, just 6 years later, Google introduced automated cars that did exactly that. The point is that progress in information technology is very likely to exceed our expectations and surprise us in the coming years.<br /><br />While the problems are laid out clearly, I think the solutions offered are pretty conventional. The authors' call for reforming and upgrading schools, for example, is something that just about everyone can agree on. However, even if we managed to do that (and we are not making much progress), those kids would not enter the workforce for many years, and who knows what technology will be capable of by then?<br /><br />Our children will face an entirely new job market and economy. Everyone should really read both \\"Race Against the Machine and also another important book, \\"The Lights in the Tunnel\\".?<br /><br />Our children will face an entirely new job market and economy. Everyone should really read both \\"Race Against the Machine and \\"The Lights in the Tunnel\\".

B005WTR4ZI|5.0|This book does three important things.  First, without hype or bias it confirms the role of information technology as a driver of current economic disruptions (unemployment, income disparity, corporate profit), a secular force like the steam engine and electricity.  Second, it convincingly predicts we ain't seen nothing yet: Moore's law for the driver sets the pace of economic disruption for the forseeable future.  Gone should be any discussion in a firm or a government about performance and strategy that does not include IT as a root cause.   Third, it offers advice for fixes: some broad, like education reform, and some specific examples for what should be done.  The specifics are necessarily incomplete and sketchy. Some look undoable currently.  No presidential candidate is likely to win advocating them.  But they illustrate new priorities for business and government attention given the importance of the IT driver of change.  Overall, my MIT colleagues' messages are a vital guide to CEOs' and politicians' decisions, based on sound economic principles and fact-based analysis.

Even Alien3 , dark and dreary as that one is, sometimes feels like a better film.<br /><br />I suppose you *could* describe this as some kind of Vietnam parable, but it's hard to take that seriously when this movie runs like a war movie propaganda love-fest of the military, fetishistically obsessing over weapons and firepower to the point of designing the darn spaceship to look like a mutated \\"pulse rifle.\\" I'd like to say that James Cameron might be a bit embarrassed by this a little bit by now, but \\"Avatar\\" is proof positive that his love affair with guns and the military is still going strong.<br /><br />It's a very naive, over-hyped Reagan-era attitude to guns and firepower which gets in the way of character and atmosphere and depth. (And compare all of this with Weaver's gun control politics of the time, and you'd understand her reservations about using every type of firepower in the last third of the film.) It's just near-brainless wall-to-wall action with the occasional bits of terror and suspense. Composer James Horner is at his laziest (or, to be fair, his most rushed) here, using the most militaristic aspects of his Trek movie scores and hyper-brassing and -percussioning them through the stratosphere. 2001's Khatchaturian piece is reprised, one cue from the first film is put in (the only good part, ironically), and the climax to the first Star Wars' Battle of Yavin sequence is blatantly ripped off (and ironically, insultingly used in hundreds of SF/suspense/action movie trailers for years thereafter). The rest of Horner's score is just okay, searching for but never finding an original note or phrase or style to present.<br /><br />Also, this is the only Alien movie sullied by a happy ending, making a jarring shift of tone from the other three films. Oh, gee, look, the happy, valiant standard nuclear family survives, complete with pieces of their best robot pal to play with. (Yeah, I know, he's an android.) Maybe they could've gotten away with this ending, but did they have to be so insanely saccharine about it?!<br /><br />Yeah, \\"Aliens\\" is a nice film, but it never reaches the sheer quality of the first one, and it doesn't age well.in (the only good part, ironically), and the climax to the first Star Wars' Battle of Yavin sequence is blatantly ripped off (and ironically, insultingly used in hundreds of SF/suspense/action movie trailers for years thereafter). 0316017930|4.0|Outliers is written to help us understand why some people are successful.  Outliers are considered to be out of the norm.  Success is more than mere intelligence.  External factors such as school, chance, parenting, timing, culture, and date of birth are jus a few of the ideas tat Gradwell discusses.  Gladwell states that outliers are \\"invariably the beneficiaries of hidden advantages that allow them to learn and work hard and make sense of the world in ways others cannot.  Gladwell insists that lower-income students would have equally measurable test scores as those of influence.  If those same lower income students had the after school and summer opportunities of the more fortunate, then their test scores would be compatible.  The author believes that once IQ is determined to be at a threshold of 115, then anything over that number is irrelevant to the level of success of the individual.  For example, African-American students were accepted into Harvard Law School because of affirmative action.  Their test scores were lower than those of more affluent-accepted with higher test scores.  At graduation, those African-American students' performance was still lower than the others.  However, all were equally successful.  The smarter students were not more successful in the professional world when all were \\"smart\\" to begin with.  You've always heard, \\"practice makes perfect.\\"  Gladwell proposes and confirms the 10,000 hour rule.  He believes that it takes a minimum of 10,000 hours or 10 years of practice to achieve success in a profession, trade of any field.  It has to be deliberate practice.  The idea that excellence at performing a complete task requires a critical minimum level of practice surfaces again and again studies of expertise.  This is the 10,000 hours.  Bill Gates, Bill Joy, The Beatles, and Mozart are a few of the examples used by the author to prove his 10,000 hour theory.  Another factor of success is when you are born.  This is chance.  Depending on your birth month, you may have an \\"accumulative advantage\\" in particular situations.  One example is Canadian Hockey players born in the first three months of the year.  They have the advantage of physical and emotional maturity.  The players who went on to be high performing, successful players had a common denominator.  They were born in January, February, March, or April.  Being born in the early part of the year was an advantage for these boys.  This carries over into education.  Birthdates determines when you start kindergarten.  This varies by state; however, the theory is the same.  Those born after the cut-off date have the advantage compared to those who will be nearly one year younger when starting kindergarten.  Gladwell focuses on cultures at the end of his book.  He emphasis the Southern tradition and their culture of honor.  He notes the Asian culture and how the simplicity of their number language allows them to develop a deeper understanding of number sense.  Likewise, the Chinese excel in Math because they have achieved the 10,000 hour rule at an early age.  Another culture of interest is the Korean culture regarding flight safety.  The culture legacy was that you respected and honored authority.  The Koreans use the \\"mitigated speech,\\" which refers to any attempt to downplay or sugarcoat the meaning of what is being said \\"had to be offered for safety reasons.\\"  In conclusion, from an educator's standpoint, Outliers is a thought-provoking read recommended for anyone of any age or profession. Who's-sleeping-with-who is left mostly hinted-at and understated, which is a little annoying, but at the same time a nice change from the usual fare, forcing the viewer to pay attention to subtleties and work it out for themselves.  I didn't even realize until afterward that the ghost writer had never been introduced by name - which was a clever twist in and of itself.  He really is a man without an identity of his own, and even in this story he takes on the role of his predecessor.  The timing of scenes and events throughout the storyline is perfect, and the gray, rain-drenched atmosphere really adds to the overall feel of the story.  Likewise for the sterile, cold, industrial-modern home where much of the action takes place: a mansion full of glass walls that leave the characters looking exposed to the elements, but none the less hide the secrets within - and a groundskeeper who's constantly sweeping leaves off the walkway, which promptly get blown back.  There's a metaphor in there somewhere.  The solution to the mystery is elegant and unexpected, and you get an inkling of it right about the same time as the ghost writer does.  It's brilliant and inevitable enough to make some of the earlier scenes fall into place in a whole new light.  My main quibble is with the ending, which did not grow out of the plot the way everything else did, but instead was gratuitous, disconnected, and completely unnecessary, and ruined what would otherwise have been a great little movie.  It was like the director wanted to inject some totally random negative element at the end, in a misguided attempt to be \\"artsy.\\"  It's still a good movie - but not one to bother watching a second time.

Although this is true to some degree, the author presented enough information in the interviews to reveal the \\"real\\" people behind the narrative. At any rate, this isn't the sort of book that lends itself well to character development, and that wasn't a negative for me.<br /><br />Some gore, some bad language, no sex. The title of my review reveals everything this book provided; it was entertaining and terrifying. I recommend it.ional. B005BDZLYQ|5.0|With no disrespect toward Hawk, Bean, Prez, Trane, Rollins, Getz, Shorter, Henderson, Dexter and Brecker, Hank Mobley is the tenor player I listen to more than any other (were Sonny Stitt exclusively a tenor player, his recordings would be a close second, with Harold Land, Charlie Rouse, Oliver Nelson and Paul Gonsalves in the 3rd spot).  Mobley doesn't so much &quot;impress&quot; as &quot;seduce&quot; the listener with ceaselessly melodic, lyrical, soulful inventions each time out.  He was no &quot;innovator&quot; or trailblazer.  Nor, like so many &quot;showier&quot; tenors, did he introduce &quot;artifacts&quot; into his sound--wobbles, growls, squeals and screeches, etc., approaches as common during the '30s and '40s as in the adventurous experimentation of modal and free players in the '60s and beyond.<br /><br />Rather, he simply took the chords that were given him and sang his heart out, all the while embodying the history and very soul of this African-American art form.  Of all tenor players, he &quot;wears&quot; the best, perhaps because he's so human, so sympathetic, so personal.  If his ideas are &quot;understated&quot; compared to those of his more dramatic, sensational, trendsetting, head-turning peers, that disarming quality is key to his appeal, which for the past 40 or more years, has never lessened for this listener. Unlike Bird and Coltrane, Hank can't be considered one of the &quot;seminal&quot; greats: he's not one of the 5-6 jazz &quot;immortals.&quot;  But for this listener no one personifies what the music is all about better than the always welcome, unobtrusive yet inimitable, voice of Mobs.<br /><br />Mobley is heard at his best for the ten years between 1955 and 1965 (his decline, due to personal as well as marketplace factors, is at once precipitous and painful to experience. The last time I caught him live--in the '70s--he was incapable of physical composure let alone constructing a musical solo). The present collection--a new approach to winning listeners back to CDs, giving them 8 for the price of one--is a better representation than that of some of the other artists to receive the same value packaging (e.g. Kenny Dorham) .  It includes perhaps his two most essential albums under his own name--critically acclaimed sessions like &quot;Soul Station&quot; and &quot;Roll Call&quot; and mostly-overlooked dates like the 4-tenor blowing session featuring Mobley with Coltrane, Al Cohn and Zoot Sims (Mobley's show-stopping solo and cadenza on &quot;How Deep Is the Ocean&quot; helps compensate for the recorded moment on Miles' &quot;Someday My Prince Will Come,&quot; on Columbia, when he was blindsided and virtually humiliated by Coltrane).<br /><br />Some essential Mobley dates are missing--including &quot;Work Out&quot; and &quot;Another Work Out.&quot;  And, of course, some of Mobley's very best playing (about half of my Mobley collection) is on recordings under the leadership of another musician--usually Art Blakey or Horace Silver.  Finally, my very favorite Mobley solo is not on a Blue Note or Prestige album but a two-disc Miles Davis session on Columbia: &quot;Friday and Saturday Night at the Blackhawk.&quot;  Following Miles' two laconic, low-key choruses on &quot;Bye Bye Blackbird&quot; Mobley simply pours his heart out for four 32-bar choruses, each more inventive, lyrical and passionate than the preceding one.  A desert-island solo if there ever was one.<br /><br />But there's no possibility of getting too much of Mobley (at least before 1966), which makes this a package that's easy to greet with a high-five, whether for the newbie or the Mobley convert who has the above &quot;missing&quot; albums and wishes to complete a Mobley collection while saving shelf-space and quite a few bucks.winning listeners back to CDs, giving them 8 for the price of one--is a better representation than that of some of the other artists to receive the same value packaging (e.g. Kenny Dorham) . 0553807919|5.0|This book is based on the concept that to deal with your child - and to help them thrive - it is helpful to understand how a child's brain works. It is such a simple, yet powerful, premise. While it focuses on ages 0-12, the concepts can be adapted to apply to teens, and even adults.<br /><br />So, why do parents, teachers, grandparents, etc, need a book like this? The different parts of our brains often don't work together very well. Think of when people get scared and can't be calmed, even though they logically know that there is no real threat. This is an example of the older parts of the brain overpowering the more developed parts. Unfortunately for parents and caregivers, the older parts often overpower the more developed parts, because a child's brain is not fully developed until the mid-20s. Also, the hemispheres of our brains handle situations differently, and if children are actively using one side over the other, we need to engage the hemisphere they are working from. This book will show you how to interact with children to help them become more calm, flexible, and balanced by taming the reptilian brain (where tantrums emerge), engaging the advanced areas of their brain, and helping them integrate their left and right hemispheres.<br /><br />For example, when a child is emotionally upset (using the right brain), many parents try logical, left-brain solutions, a \\"dismiss and deny\\" approach (\\"there is no good reason to cry, so stop!\\"). A better response to an upset child would be to \\"connect and redirect,\\" i.e. to acknowledge their feelings (\\"yes, that is upsetting\\"), comfort them physically (e.g. with a hug), and later approach the situation more logically. This works for adults too, since when we are upset, angry, etc, most of us rarely become calm after someone screams at us or tells us to \\"just stop\\" something. The book contains many other useful tips to help parents deal with the day-to-day headaches...I mean...joys of children.<br /><br />As a parent of a young child, I was fascinated. The book is interesting and filled with helpful advice that is similar to strategies I have put into practice in my own life to become happier and more balanced. It contains many entertaining stories, and the comic strips that pop up throughout helped me understand the material better. The end material is a helpful summary of the advice contained in the book. I have been reading on a variety of brain-related topics lately, and they all seem to be integrated in this one book! I see instances of Neuro-linguistic Programming, Mindfulness, and Non-Violent Communication (Empathetic Communication). It is really cool to see all of these excellent theories and resources applied to parenting. I would highly recommend this book. It is based on accepted and emerging brain science and provides an alternative to the \\"yell and yell more\\" technique that so many of us have used (and had used on us) with ineffective results. Will every solution in the book work every time? Probably not, but there are some great ideas to try!inated. I went from a size 2 to a plump size 6 or from a muscular 130 pounds to a pudgy 145 pounds. I would like to say that my weight bothered me, but it did not. It did, however, bother my doctor. I had become pre-diabetic and she wanted me to begin taking metformin. This freaked me out more than a little bit--I am 37 years old; that is too young to begin popping pills. I had never been on a diet before and after reading this book I never will be. This is what I gleaned from Mr. Taubes' treatise: I could count calories and begin a vicious cycle of deprivation and hope I was successful or I could count carbs (junky carbs had to go, period). It has been 6 months since I read this book and I have read The Paleo Diet and The Atkins book since then so I may be mixing my content up, but this book sparked a lifestyle change for me. I decided to follow the Atkins' lifestyle, rather than the Paleo, because I still like junkfood and the Paleo was pretty hardcore, but I wouldn't have even looked at either of those books if it weren't for this book. I lost 10% of my body weight in 7 weeks (beginning 06/04/11) and my weight has since stabilized. My blood sugar is beautiful and I am back in my size 2 jeans and skirts. I don't work out, which does not make my doctor happy, but I am taking a baby steps attitude towards all this. Incidentally, my husband, who is very supportive, decided to eat the low carb way as well. He is 6'3\\" and weighed 250 when we began; he is 215 now and still losing weight although he doesn't need to. This book is a life changer; I can't say how happy I am that I bought it.although he doesn't need to. This book is a life changer; I can't say how happy I am that I bought it.

1439153663|4.0|My Review:   The Kitchen House by Kathleen Grissom was selected by my book group, Books & Babble for our April discussion. We met last week to discuss the book. The Kitchen House is the story of life on a southern Plantation in the 1780's. The story is told by alternating narrators, Ladvinia and Belle. Belle is a slave on the plantation who is also the daughter of the Captain. Ladvinia is a young Irish girl who comes to the plantation at the age of 7 when the Captain purchases her as on indentured servant. Having two narrators worked with this story as Ladvinia's observations of events were usually immature and na√Øve. Belle, was wise and understood life on the Plantation in a way that Ladvinia did not grasp.  There are many themes within The Kitchen House such as the theme of history repeating itself and effects of isolation on the people of the plantation. The other theme all too common in this dark time of history is the disregard of the person of color as anything more than an object, owned by their masters. I am always bothered by how any human being can treat other human being so brutally. I am truly appalled by this time in our nation's history.  One member of my book group shared that her relative was an indenture servant as a small child, coming to the Midwest on the Orphan train from New York. It was very interesting to hear about this and luckily the small child was treated very well and loved. I hope our nation takes the time to learn from it's history and not repeat it. But then again don't get me started on how our nation treats the less fortunate. I am a social worker so I do have a bit of an opinion on the subject. I won't use this platform for a political rant.  The flow of The Kitchen House is very smooth, even with the transition between the two narrators. The book was very engaging and kept my attention. It had me gasping out loud at points and as well as cheering for a revolution. I found myself sneaking off to read a few more pages whenever I could.  The characters are well developed. You hate the bad characters and love the good ones. It seems like in The Kitchen House you were either good or bad, there was little in between. I loved Belle, her strength and wisdom are evident throughout the book. I also loved the fact that she loved her son with all her heart despite the events that lead to his conception. Mama and Papa were wise and honest slaves that loved Ladvinia as their own. They protected her, the best they could even when her own actions put them in harm's way. I loved Will and his treatment of the workers and the way he ran the plantation with compassion and efficiently.  I was sad for the Kitchen House to end; I wanted to know what happened next to the characters I started to care so much about.   My Rating: 4/5 - very good book - highly recommend.

B0053YGYO4|5.0|Britpop can be a bit of a wide ranging category, but I look for guitar driven pop with clear vocals and excellent melodies.  Coldplay's album output has helped to define the 2000's definition of Britpop--they have brought it away from the 90's Oasis/Suede blueprint, and more towards a midpoint between Radiohead and Keane.  On this album, they have indulged their poppier side of things.  Melodies are sumptuous, uplifting, big chorused affairs with ringing, chiming guitars supported by rainbow waterfalls of electronics and strings.  Chris Martin's vocals are amazing, high, powerful, clear, and beautiful.  Of course, they have also been multi-tracked and backed, and as a result, his voice sounds almost orchestral at times.  All of this together is blended in an extremely sophisticated fashion, and from a sheer sonic perspective, it is an absolute triumph.  Of course, all the fantastic production in the world would not be worth a whit if the songs didn't back it up--but they do.  You've probably heard Paradise and Every Teardrop is a waterfall by now, and they are wonderful, full of colors shifting everywhere, so beautiful and magical.  There are intimate moments (like the amazing \\"Us Against the World\\") that reinforce the well known fact that Chris Martin wears his heart on his sleeve, and they deliver on the promise of songs like \\"Sparks\\" from the old days of Parachutes.  There are some surprises--the U2 like guitar break in M.M.I.X. is mesmerising.  Synths in Princess of China stutter along with a definite 80's feel for a few moments before they are subsumed in the cascading production once more, but they don't feel out of place, instead they are well integrated and wonderful.  Don't Let It Break Your Heart, towards the end of the album is so beautiful that I had to stop listening for a moment, recompose myself, and start it again.  I know, overdramatic, but I guess I was just in the right moment for that song.  I think that that is one of the best things about thisalbum--there are so many little surprises that make it something special, so many moments when all the sweetness, innocence, beauty, heartbreak, and wonder all come together.  It's hard for me to know yet if this is my favorite Coldplay album--currently, that spot is still occupied by X&Y--but it is a question that I look forward to exploring for quite a while coming.  I'm clearing my schedule to spend a lot of time with this disk.  I think we are going to be good friends for a long, long time.  Highly recommended.

Major Minus: 5/5 Finally, something weird and different! I love this song, I have since I first bought it on the ETiaW single. Its an odd acoustic song for them, and again Buckland is amazing. The vocal sound is weird and haunting, and on topof that the lyrics are reminiscent of the White Rose movement in Nazi Germany. Interesting, fun, dark song. I've always thought Coldplay should delve into a darker sound due to their overly-poppy sound, and this just furthers my thinking.  9. U.F.O.: 4/5 Another acoustic song that reminds us of previous works. Rihanna is not to be blamed for this track, but she also shouldn't have phoned it in. She sounds like the popular girl in high school that is at the dance with the nerdiest guy because she lost a bet. And it doesn't end like most of those stories do, there's not happy ending on this song. Chris Martin isn't even trying here. Up in Flames: 3/5 This song has potential. Its extremely minimalist and while its got boringly easy lyrics they're delivered in an interesting way. The only other detractor is that the bass drum kick is super electronic. It hurts the song. Stick with real instruments guys. Other than that this isn't a bad song at all. Not amazing, but ok. I think it will be remembered as a low point by most but I enjoyed it after the electro-drowned Princess of China.  12. Don't Le it Break Your Heart: 3/5 Despite my hatred of the over-excess of electronics on this album I can't help but kind of enjoy this song a bit. Its not terrible, its fun, and it keeps you interested. This is also some of the better vocal work from Chris on the album, and that seems long overdue at this point.  14. Up With the Birds: 4/5 Borrowing a lot of lyrics from Leonard Cohen in the most shameless display of repackaging I've ever seen, this song is supposed to be the happy ending to the album. If it had been more original it might be my favorite song on the album but I can't get past that. They've done that a lot on this album and its a major negative point but at this point its just another disappointment.  This album will be hailed as visionary and amazing, but I'm going to go ahead and call it a failure. There are good parts of this album. There are bad parts of this album. Sadly, the bad parts will overshadow the good parts for many people. This is a sad attempt after the highly experimental Viva la Vida and with their patter hopefully that means the next album will be amazing. Parachutes was ok, then A Rush of Blood was amazing. X&Y was ok but it was overdone stadium rock meant to appeal and recreate previous successes but then Viva was a complete overhaul. Now there's this, where it appears they've given up on doing something revolutionary and gone for appealing to the lowest common denominator. Its still Coldplay, but its kind of bland for them. Hopefully they bounce back eventually.

0345472322|4.0|The book Mindset is a good read for all people, whether you are a business leader, an athlete, or even an educator. It was a very encouraging read as it helped provide examples of people who overcame adversity and struggles only to later become well known success stories. Dweck defines the two mindsets: the fixed mindset and the growth mindset, discusses how the mindset one has will affect the amount of success they will achieve, and how one can go about changing their mindset.   One particularly inspiring individual with a growth mindset is Wilma Rudolph. Rudolph was constantly sick and nearly died as a result of several illnesses.  The reason for her success was because she possessed a growth mindset that pushed her to practice and grow, rather than give up. This story was inspiring as a teacher because many students today come from backgrounds that may hinder their success.   Dweck informs the reader of several individuals who exhibit a fixed mindset. Individuals with a fixed mindset believe that talents and abilities are static, without the possibility for change or growth. Those with the fixed mindset are perceived as \\"naturals.\\" One of these is John McEnroe. Dweck engages in a careful description of how his fixed mindset ultimately led to failure.   The growth mindset can be learned with a conscientious effort. Everyday our minds take into account what has gone on throughout the day. The fixed mindset people focuses solely on the mistakes made throughout the day whereas the growth mindset individuals look at the mistakes and analyze how they can learn from the their mistakes. In order to go from a fixed mindset to a growth mindset it's all about altering your way of thinking. Instead of thinking of your mistakes as failures, thinking of it as a learning experience. This will allow for more personal growth as well as higher levels of achievement.  Overall, Dweck's Mindset leaves readers with a passion to make a change. This change can be made in all aspects oflife. She provides clear steps on how to shift fixed-mindset thinking to growth-mindset thinking. She also provides examples to assist the reader in understanding the positive impact that shifting mindsets can make on one's life.   The most important take away from this read is that fixed-mindset individuals often focus on appearing perfect. This is often manifested in \\"low-effort syndrome.\\" Students with low-effort syndrome typically refrain from trying in an effort to not appear dumb. For teachers of gifted students, this is especially important. Dweck believes that in order to push a student out of the growth mindset a teacher has to become a nurturer and focus more on student growth over grades. This point is important because far too often do school systems and teachers focus on the results of tests not a student's effort and beginning to end development.

MFSL's Original Master Recording editions were:  -Direct from the Original Master Recording, under strict licence.  -Mastered at Half Speed, and De-Burred, before beginning the limited pressings.   -Pressed on Super Slick [therefore quiet] Translucent virgin vinyl.  -After the limited pressing the master is destroyed to prevent further pressings.  Several 'Hits' sprung forth from The Doors [album] as this track list attests:  SIDE ONE  \\"Break on Through (To the Other Side)\\" - 2:29[Hit]  \\"Soul Kitchen\\" - 3:35  \\"The Crystal Ship\\" - 2:34  \\"Twentieth Century Fox\\" - 2:33  \\"Alabama Song (Whisky Bar)\\" (Bertolt Brecht, Kurt Weill) - 3:20[Hit]  \\"Light My Fire\\" - 7:06Studio edition[Hit: A Song the Band performed live on Ed Sullivan]  SIDE TWO  \\"Back Door Man\\" (Willie Dixon) - 3:34  \\"I Looked at You\\" - 2:22  \\"End of the Night\\" - 2:52  \\"Take It as It Comes\\" - 2:23  \\"The End\\" - 11:41[Hit: Used in Apocalypse Now]  Even the Compact Disc release of The Doors [album] DOES NOT include the rich tones of this release. LP vinyl was was the intended medium of it's delivery to the music buying public. They stretched it to the limit. Both in Quantity and Quality of the production. To hear it with Minimal tracking noise and pops and ticks is awesome.  This edition stands as THE version needed to enjoy some nice 60's, Psychedelic/Rock.
6302642175|5.0|Those of us who were lucky enough to see the original premier of the first Star Wars film in the 1970's were amazed at the high level of realism created by the new technological innovation of computer animation. What was also surprising at that time was that this new advance came not from Walt Disney Studios, but from a new group called Industrial Light and Magic. As ILM developed the technology over the course of each new Star Wars movie, Disney looked to the future and decided to produce a computer animated film of its own, Tron. The great Disney artists showed how beautifully they could use the new medium by creating a vision of a living world within a mainframe computer--more than a decade before the Matrix trilogy. In the story the Master Control program of a computer technologies company gains power by drawing all programs within the mainframe into itself. Its counterpart in the real world is an ambitious programmer who rises to the company's top position by pirating the work of a video games innovator. The sinister Master Control program uses this knowledge to gain a foothold in the real world. The only barrier in its path to control of both virtual and real worlds is the video game wizard, Flynn, played by Jeff Bridges. Master Control uses an experimental program that transposes matter to electrons to pull Flynn into the mainframe world. His plan is to destroy the programmer on the game grid. But Flynn is the game master and escapes to help the beings that inhabit the digital landscape regain their freedom, as well as save the real world from the MCP's plans. Although the story is simple, it is told with a visual complexity that is still a delight to the eye after 30 or so years. Tron maintained enough popularity over the years as a cult classic to inspire a sequal over 3 decades later, Tron Legacy. This is a must-have video for anyone who is a collector of films that are landmarks in the industry.

B0053O8AQE|4.0|Even in unfortunate situations like the recent scandals that have rocked Wall Street, and affected the future of millions of investors, there are opportunities to create humor.  Created by Michael Patrick King and Whitney Cummings, CBS's 2 Broke Girls, is a comedy set in New York City featuring two women from completely different ends of the socioeconomic spectrum, who come together as coworkers, only because one has lost her fortune, due to her father's involvement in a financial scandal.  This is a well-cast show with pretty good writing, but it is often a little too crude than it needs to be, with frequent references to sex, drugs, and crime.<br /><br />The series stars Kat Demmings as Max Black, a waitress in a small restaurant in Brooklyn.  She's street smart, a little hardened and cynical, and has a bit of a gutter mouth, but underneath that rough and crude exterior, there's a warm caring person.  Beth Behrs is Caroline Channing, a slim blonde beauty who bears more than a passing resemblance to Paris Hilton.  Caroline's father Martin was convicted and sent to prison for his involvement in a financial scandal (think Bernie Madoff).  In the process, the Channing family's financial assets were frozen, leaving Caroline locked out of her luxury apartment, and denied access to her rather substantial trust fund.<br /><br />The setup for the series is established in the first episode, as a desperate Caroline becomes a waitress at the Williamsburg Diner.  Owner Han Lee (Matthew Moy) hires Caroline, teaming her with Max, who is less than enthusiastic with being saddled with someone with zero experience.  Also in the restaurant are Oleg (Jonathan Kite) a cook who sexually harasses the girls, and Earl (Garrett Morris, the venerable SNLer) the lovable cashier.  With no place to live, Max invites Caroline to stay in the apartment that she currently shares with her boyfriend, but after catching him with another woman, Max kicks him out, and Caroline becomes her new roommate.<br /><br />Much of the humor is based on the obvious and pronounced differences between the two girls.  The contrast between the women's attitudes extends far beyond just economic matters, and provides many comic opportunities for the writers.  The writing is generally sharp edged, creative, and pretty clever, although presumably to appeal to a younger audience, heavily infused with vulgarity.  The sexual innuendo is frequently at the gutter level, when it really doesn't need to be.<br /><br />Both lead actresses are well cast, and do a nice job of immersing themselves into their characters.  Kat Demmings ([[ASIN:B001PNV9FG Nick & Norah's Infinite Playlist]], Thor) brings to Max, a realistic working class toughness, surly attitude, a body loaded with ample curves, and often delivers her lines with a cynical scowl.  Behrs has good comic instincts, to go along with a model's figure, and the stereotypical airhead chick voice she employs.  Caroline is a cartoonish character, who sometimes seems semi-detached from reality.  A graduate of Wharton business school, Caroline is particularly bright when it comes to financial matters, and applying her academic rich girl sensibilities to real world situations, is one of the foundations of the program's humor.<br /><br />While the differences between the Caroline and Max establishes a baseline, it is the things that they share in common, and the mutual experiences that bond them together, that makes the show interesting, and gives it some warmth as their friendship grows.  One common interest they share early on, is Caroline's horse Chestnut, who is kept in the backyard of Max's apartment.  The big thing though is their business partnership, as the ladies plan to make a successful business from Max's talent for baking cupcakes.  At the close of each episode, the running total of their financial assets is updated.<br /><br />The characters Han, Oleg, and Earl, are a bit racially stereotyped, and as the season progresses only Oleg really assumes a more significant role.  To perk things up, late in the season the writers introduce Sofi Kachinsky (Jennifer Coolidge), a mysterious woman who lives upstairs in the same building as the girls.  Another ethnically influenced character, Sofi is from Eastern Europe and runs a cleaning service.  Sofi becoming romantically involved with Oleg, provides another bridge between the girl's private life, and the things that happen at work.  The voluptuous Jennifer Coolidge ([[ASIN:B0040BJH36 ExTerminators]]) is an experienced actress, whose presence strengthens the program, while creating more opportunities for the writers.<br /><br />With a talented and funny cast, 2 Broke Girls is clearly a series with much potential, however the writing exhibits a fixation with profanity, sexual innuendo, and double entendre, that seems to increase as the season progresses.  Even if an increase in vulgarity contributes to the program's popularity, CBS isn't a cable network, and not sinking into the gutter too often, may in the long run, result in the show reaching a larger audience.  The hilarious season finale, which features a guest appearance by Martha Stewart, is absolutely fabulous, and demonstrates that a little more class, and a little less crass, might be a winning approach for this very entertaining program.g really assumes a more significant role. B005WTR4ZI|5.0|Brynjolfsson and McAfee crisply present a hypothesis on America's jobless recovery, declining median income, and growing income disparity - and point their finger at structural changes induced by the Moore's Law growth and application of computer technology to core economic activities including finance, manufacturing, and sales. This is an important book that should be read by interested citizens as well as economists, business executives and politicians who aim to keep their jobs.<br /><br />Their hypothesis isn't based on a cheerleading hurrah for technology, but thoughtful observation on how computer based capabilities - doubling at Moore's law rates for decades - have enabled changes to the operational models of business, and the economic effect of these changes.<br /><br />Examples (some of are mine) include: efficient and systematically optimized offshore production combined with low cost and high volume sales (think Walmart, Home Depot); disintermediation or destruction of local business (add bookstores and specialty merchants versus Amazon); Securities trading and hedge funds dominated by millisecond response trading programs; Creation, packaging and sale of financial instruments that are intentionally too complex and systematically risky to understand, but far too easy to sell in a deregulated environment (from Enron to Credit Default Swaps, to Occupy Wall Street).<br /><br />Computer technology investments improve productivity - but the workers who are fired or downsized go beyond the good paying and secure manufacturing jobs which built a strong middle class in developed nations through the end of the last century. Displaced workers include local merchants, sales people, bank tellers, clerks and more. They cite John Markoff's March 2011 NYT story where \\"according to one estimate, moving from human to digital labor during the discovery process could let one lawyer do the work of 500.\\"<br /><br />The cost savings of increased productivity are real, as are the innovations in core processes - but they can increase rather than decrease middle class employment, increase income disparity, and in the financial sector increase the dominance of institutions too big to fail.<br /><br />For banking and finance, you only have to listen to Occupy Wall Street - or elements of the Tea Party - to recognize the pain caused by a combination of sorcerer's apprentice financial mechanics and trading in an era when many of the most important regulatory controls have been lifted. The transfer of wealth from mortgage holders, governments, and citizens to financial institutions too big to fail and those who benefited from that bubble beggars the imagination and the economy.<br /><br />This is a hypothesis, not (yet) presentable as economist's model - but it identifies structural elements that put computer technology squarely in the class of what economists call General Purpose Technologies (GPT) \\"... a small group of technological innovations so powerful that they interrupt and accelerate the normal march of economic progress. Steam power, electricity, and the internal combustion engine are examples of previous GPTs.\\" Like frogs in the pot of water on the stove, we have been caught unaware by the effects of enabling technology that doubles at a Moore's law rate - and it's uncomfortably hot now.<br /><br />Brynjolfsson and McAfee end with cautiously optimistic notes on how GPT capabilities eventually lead to increased competition and broadly based economic growth. That's what has happened in the past.<br /><br />Unfortunately, I believe the economic situation and the US's venal politics are also consistent with a \\"Snowcrash\\" future for the United States. In 1992, Neal Stephenson's novel was set in a Los Angeles no longer part of what is left of the United States with \\"... only four things we do better than anyone else: music, movies, microcode (software), and high-speed pizza delivery.\\"<br /><br />In my opinion, if Brynjolfsson and McAfee areright, we need to go back to Adam Smith and walk it forward again, looking back to the Standard Oil and railroad trustbusting era of Teddy Roosevelt to get out of our current finance and banking mess which holds us all at its mercy. Read this book.andard Oil and railroad trustbusting era of Teddy Roosevelt to get out of our current finance and banking mess which holds us all at its mercy. Read this book.
