B004WYT06U|4.0|Daniel Pink's A whole new mind: Why right-brainers will rule the future is an excellent read for people who are concerned about being successful in a changing world that includes emerging technologies, outsourcing, and market crowding.  Pink's unique, interesting writing style and clear organization synthesizes into an excellent book outlining how America is changing markets.  Pink opens with the unusual scene of getting his brain scanned.  From here he highlights and clarifies the roles of the left and right sides of the brain; the left is for rational, logical thinking while the right recognizes relationships and emotions.  From there, he writes about forces including abundance (mega consumers), Asia(outsourcing), and automation (technology) that are changing the job market from needing left brain directed thinking to more right brain directed thinking.  Pink explains that the previous mantra of go to school, get a degree, and become an accountant/doctor/engineer/software programmer is no longer enough for the emerging market.  Everyone has access to skill sets that can be handled by the left side of the brain, but people now need to go a step further and also incorporate the right side of their brain.  Basically, if you are worried that your job could be replaced by a computer or someone in Asia, then you are in serious trouble. But do not fear, Pink provides the six senses that will save you in this upcoming move into what he dubbed the Conceptual Age: design, story, symphony, empathy, play, and meaning.  Pink declares that design can change the world, a bold statement until he reminds us of the impact of design on the outcome of the Gore Bush election.  The ability to create an effective narrative (story), make novel connections (symphony), and analyze for deeper understanding (meaning) are also imperative to being successful.  These three senses/abilities are distinctive to individuals; only you might be able to create the perfect context in a narrative, makethat new connection, or see the big picture.  Pink also mentions the importance of being able to empathize and play (two characteristics that computers do not have) will also help set you a part and become successful in the Conceptual Age.  Overall, Pink is emphasizing senses that will engender your individual potential.  Pink incorporates a lot of humor and interesting situations on his journey through each sense.  He travels to India to visit a laughter club, explores how the military utilizes video games, attended an intense drawing class, visited a public design school in inner city Philadelphia, walked a labyrinth, volunteered to be a part of a medical experiment, and incorporated several interesting and illuminating images.  All six of these senses are needed to become successful in this new Conceptual Age, and Pink generously supplies the reader with portfolios which map out how to master each sense.  Most include small suggestions such as readings or keeping a journal, while other include more involved measures such as finding and attending a laughter club yourself.  According to Pink, if you master each of these senses, you will be prepared to not only survive this new Conceptual Age, but be prosperous.

It's not all that common, or at least it don't appear to me to be so, but it truly applies here, \\"There is Metal, and then there is Metallica.\\"   This Album does so well, that just a few years ago, it got the 5X platinum rating which is amazing for an album released in 84, now how many albums from how many bands can do that? I personally feel that this album while loved to death by Metallica Fans, especially those who favor Cliff-Era prefer this one over all others, and I do so myself, but it seems that the more casual fans of Metallica get Black, Justice for all, and Master of Puppets before looking at this one. I believe that is a mistake, because this is the most perfect thrash album in their entire collection. It's closer to thrash than any other in their collection and yet holds amazing melodic beauty that is sometimes lacking in Puppets, but found and so loved in parts of Black. This is Metallica, through and through. Now the songs.   *Fight Fire with Fire* starts out with an unusual slow almost like rock kind of strumming that is designed to lull you into one feeling, and then blow you away with the awesome metal that erupts from it's very core of awesome. First thing I notice, is the sound is a major improvement over Kill Em All, because that one did so well they were able to get better quality crew to help turn out their sound, plus Kirk, James, and Lars all show great improvement. About mid point it jumps more instrumental, including the minute long Solo by Kirk before finally it goes back to James to bring it home, which it does well with a great shorter instrumental part near the end, before going into the last verse and then finishing up with a long but great closer instrumentation. This song is just awesome, someone wants an example of Thrash, Play ride the lightning, it's not the fastest Metallica song, but I dare call it one of the best of Metallica's more Thrash type songs.   *For Whom the Bell Tolls* A requirement for all Metallica Concerts in my opinion. This song slows down even more than the previous song, and yet is still far faster than any of the hair metal, and great on the ears to boot. This song has a hammer effect, even the drums hit that momentary hammer sound between each line of the lyric. Like Lightning, it holds a generally stable feel all the way to the end with bits of instrumentation, and solos. The song fades out in a manner as if it's winding you up for the next song, which is great. It's got some of my most favorite lyrics in the entirety of Metallica's library. Like Lightning before it, this song is one of the 3 choice songs from the album, not to say the others are bad, this whole album is great.   *Fade to Black* Was a song of such a nature in sound that when I was a little kid and didn't know no better, I thought it was on black, which is by far one of their most melodic albums, and much like the best parts of the later black album, this song is one of their best of the more melodic type songs. The previous two songs maintained a general pace, while this one slowly amps up with each moment of the song. I've heard this song so many times, and even listening to it now, it still has a powerful effect over me. At the 4 minute mark, the song changes, and it gets more intense as does the lyrics itself, and from here it starts amping up even faster until it goes intoa high speed thrash of metal glory. This is the third of the three most loved songs from this album. This is without a doubt in my opinion, one of the top 5 most beautiful songs in their entire Library.   *Trapped under Ice* this song as an odd opening for a Metallica song. Even as a Metallica fan, I'd never heard this song before until I got this album, (which was the last I got, other than Anger) but this song is all kinds of awesome, it's the fastest song on the entire album. Less notable than creeping death but in my opinion slightly better. This song has not been heard live more than a handful of times, and not until recently at that, it was played live 5 times from Lightning's release until Magnetic tour. It's unfortunate. This song would have been great on the Binge and Purge album. A great song, among Metallica's best.   *Escape* has a very off putting opening that reminds me of a video game sometimes, and I tend to avoid playing it. But it's not a bad song, I'm especially fond of the Chorus here. This song has never been played live in it's entirety. Weakest song on the album, but far better than 90% of the metal that was out there in those years, that's Metallica for you.   *Creeping Death* What's not to love? Great title, great song, awesomely fast, as I said before it's slower than trapped under ice, but that's only barely, and in this case this makes this song second fastest on the album and one of Metallica's fastest songs. My absolute favorite part of the song is the way James' vocals complement the music, and vice versa. It's seems almost in sync, by virtue of this effect, this is without a doubt the only Metallica song I don't like live, however it is very popular live, so we'll leave that is my opinion only, because this effect is very very hard to replicate, even for pros. This is one of my choice songs for a playlist while playing Gears of War.   *Call of the Ktulu* Finally, some people prefer Orion is the classic instrumental song, I don't. This song is better than Orion in my opinion, and as a result, I consider it to be their best instrumental song. It's powerful, it's amazing, it's incredible. One could argue that this is the 4th choice song from the album. The song itself due to length and complexity would be difficult to describe, so the best way to describe it, this song tells a story of sound without words in such a manner that it far surpasses many songs that tell a story in words. I've actually timed cooking food to this song, LOL. When it's done, certain meals I eat happen to be done.   The Album through and through is one of the best, if not the best Thrash metal, if not Metal album. If you love Metallica, even just the old stuff. You want this album, you need this album. 0553801473|3.0|War drags on in the Seven Kingdoms. The earlier major players are now largely dead, but their children and heirs battle on. In the north, Jon Snow is now commander of the black watch along the wall. In the trading cities of the south, Daenerys rules over theformer slave city of Meereen--and the natives are restive. Although she speaks of seizing her native kingdom, Daenerys knows that the slavers would return if she leaves, and cannot abandon her adoptive children. So, the world beats a path to Daenerys as every second son, exile, and wanna-be power on the outs makes the voyage to the woman who mothered dragons, and who has a claim to be rightful ruler of the seven kingdoms.<br /><br />George R. R. Martin's epic series A SONG OF ICE AND FIRE was eye-opening and genre-stretching. I remember reading A GAME OF THRONES practically breathless. Every plot twist (and boy did the plot twist) caught me by surprise. Every character I grew to love seemed set up to fail. Martin's strong writing continues, and he has the knack of writing characters complex enough that we care about them, seeing each one as hero of his (or her) own story. Even Queen Cersei--an adulterer, incest participant, and murderer, becomes sympathetic. Here, though, in the fifth book in the series, the characters seem smaller and more focussed on every-day details.<br /><br />Again, I think Martin does fantasy a favor by adding not only the blood and dirt, but also the reality of war and occupation. Victory is not a matter of marching armies through town in a parade, it's sullen opposition, sneak attacks, and worrying whether your allies have betrayed you (or if your dragons have been eating your peoples' children). Managing a rag-tag force on the distant border isn't all about battling giants, it's watching larders empty and worrying about starvation. That being said, we get a LOT of that kind of detail in a book that stretches for 959 pages (not including the lists of characters).<br /><br />One of the key dangers in writing series is that authors find the need to move characters from point to point. That, to a large extent, is what is happening in A DANCE WITH DRAGONS. Tyrion is traveling to meet Daenerys--but doesn't quite get there. Stannis Baratheon is traveling toward Winterfell--but doesn't quite get there. Several suitors are trying to get to Daenerys--but mostly don't get there. Arya is learning to be invisible and to be an assassin--and actually makes progress. Ultimately, I appreciate what Martin is doing and I think he's adding key elements to a genre that has, to a large extent, offered its readers easy blacks and whites, simple solutions to complex problems. But I wanted to see more happen. Fans of this series could, I fear, skip the entire 959 pages and pick up the next volume without having to scratch their heads and wonder what has changed. The answer, to a large extent, is not much.<br /><br />Okay, I'm a fan of Martin's. Martin continues to be an effective wordsmith, continues to create a wonderfully complex world, continues to frustrate (and reward) his readers by refusing to let his characters find simplistic solutions to the problems they face. Overall, fans of this series (like me) will want to read A DANCE WITH DRAGONS. There's enough here to entertain and fascinate--but I do think it could have moved a bit faster.y dangers in writing series is that authors find the need to move characters from point to point. 0439023521|4.0|Suzanne Collins combines a negative utopia, gut-wrenching action, and a fight to the death in a book that you cannot put down.  This novel opens up to the grim world of sixteen-year-old Katniss Everdeen, living in Panem, a divided nation of twelve districts.  Every year, two tributes (male and female) are selected from each district and forced participate in the Hunger Games, a gruesome fight to death televised live by the Capitol.  The tributes are forced to fight in desolate conditions, use survival skills, and in the end, the winner gets to live high on the hog. What do the losers get? Death.     After recuperating from the death of her father, Katniss uses her prior knowledge accumulated from her deceased father and manages to keep her family in a steady condition. But her feeble mother can't help her, and she finds it a waste of time to try to teach her wide-eyed, innocent, animal-loving sister Primrose to hunt.  Instead, she gets assistance from her long-time friend, hunting pal, and love interest, eighteen year-old Gale.  Trespassing and poaching are against the law; but Katniss has no alternative option.  Unless you count starving to death.    The actual Hunger Games begin with the Reaping, the most dreaded day of the year for citizens of Panem.  Each year, a female and a male tribute are selected \\"randomly\\". The age range is twelve to eighteen, and since Prim is twelve, it's the first year that her name will be entered in the pool.  Children can enter their names additionally for tesserae, one-year supply of grain and oil.  When Katniss was twelve, her name was entered once, and then three more times for tesserae for her mother, Prim, and herself. B005FITIGO|4.0|Having been the victim of corporate downsizing more than once, I was immediately engaged with this propulsive 2011 corporate drama from the beginning as Stanley Tucci's character, a seasoned risk management executive named Eric Dale, is told in a coldly indifferent manner that he is being laid off after 19 years with the same unnamed Wall Street firm. It's a piercing yet dramatically economical scene that perfectly summarizes how bloodless the corporate world can be, and in first-time writer/director J.C. Chandor's effort set on the eve of the 2008 financial crisis, it is very cold indeed with 80% of the trading floor being let go. As Dale is escorted out of the building, he hands a flash drive to his prodigious assistant Peter Sullivan and tells him to take a look at it and \\"Be careful.\\"<br /><br />Once Sullivan analyzes the data, he realizes the universal gravity of Dale's warning - that the firm is so over-committed to underwater mortgage-backed securities that the total potential loss exceeds the firm's total market capitalization value. In other words, the projected scenario means the firm will soon owe a lot more than it's worth, and the market will be on the verge of an apocalyptic meltdown. What happens after this discovery is a series of sharply intense clandestine confrontations with each level of higher-ups recognizing the ramifications of the inevitable disaster, each one far more nuanced in character than we are used to seeing in films from Oliver Stone about greed and immorality. Blessedly, Chandor doesn't stoop to the customary stereotypes in this corporate cage match, but what he does manage is capture the moral compass underneath each player by way of a cast that really delivers the goods with powerfully implosive performances.<br /><br />Zachary Quinto ([[ASIN:B002HWRYJE Star Trek]]) is initially at the center of the plot as Sullivan and performs well enough in the constraining, semi-heroic role, but the veterans really stand out here beginning with Kevin Spacey, who effectively plays against type as Sam Rogers, a genuine company man, the seen-it-all head of the trading team who rallies what's left of the trading floor with corporate brio but then faces his own cross to bear struggling to commandeer a fire sale of worthless assets dumped on unsuspecting clients. The other standout is Jeremy Irons, who masterfully resuscitates the cool cunning of his Claus von Bulow from [[ASIN:B000056BP3 Reversal of Fortune]] as the acerbically survivalist CEO John Tuld. He handily controls the boardroom scene with cutting humor and hostile precision. One of the film's more pleasant surprises is Demi Moore in cool, brisk form as Sarah Robertson, the top risk officer and lone female executive who knows her career is at stake with the discovery of this folly. Tucci is excellent in his smallish role as Dale and gets to show off his resigned character's engineering aptitude with a brief monologue about building a bridge.<br /><br />Comparatively less impressive but playing their more predictable roles fitfully are Penn Badgley as Sullivan's younger, overtly money-obsessed colleague Seth Bregman; Paul Bettany as Dale's nihilistic, snake-oil salesman of a boss, Will Emerson; and Simon Baker as the most morally despicable executive of the bunch, Jared Cohen. Mary McDonnell has a brief and frankly unnecessary scene as Rogers' ex-wife, and I didn't even recognize the usually hilarious Broadway personality Susan Blackwell as the hatchet woman in the opening scene. There are a few flaws with Chandor's observant screenplay, for example, the overly analogous scenes of Rogers dealing with his dying dog and a rooftop scene that plays up Emerson's nihilistic nature too predictably. In addition, some scenes play either too murkily or too clinically to achieve the precise dramatic effect they should. I think the absence of a musical score also contributes to the sterility of the proceedings. However, as a first-time filmmaker, Chandor more than impresses with his deft handling of such a zeitgeist moment with the Occupy Wall Street protests gaining understandable momentum right now.ling of such a zeitgeist moment with the Occupy Wall Street protests gaining understandable momentum right now.

B004XWLN20|5.0|The 10 seasons of Smallville can be broken down as follows:  Seasons 1 & 2  Clark discovers his powers and something of his origin and attempts to deal with it (remember, he is a high school frosh in the first season).  Seasons 2 (last and next to last ep), Season 3, Season 4, & Season 5 (through ep 3) Clark rebels against his destiny and Jor-el, still believing he can have some kind of normal life.  Seasons 5 (from ep 4 on), Season 6, Season 7 (ep 1) Clark resentfully and grudgingly accepts that he is not and will never be normal and gives up on normal relationships and starts to accept some parts of his supposed destiny that Jor-el and others are trying to force on him.  Season 7 (from ep 2 on), Season 8 (ep 1  Clark deals with being an adult who can no longer rely on his parents or surrogate parents and whose relationships are now adult relationships.  Season 8 (from ep 2 on), Season 9, Season 10 (through ep 11) Clark learns how to be a hero and creates his own destiny with aid from and despite Jor-el.  Season 10 (from ep 12 to the finale  Clark is effectively Superman and just has to finish up a few minor details like hiding his secret identity, his final supersuit, and flying.  Let's face it, of all the major superheroes Superman makes the least sense.  Bruce Wayne created Batman in order to terrorize criminals so they felt the fear he did when his parents were murdered.  He is motivated by revenge.  Wonder Woman was created by the Amazons in order to show their solidarity with the Allies against the Nazi threat to the world.  Peter Parker becomes Spiderman, first, to make money as a wrestler, but later as a superhero because of the guilt he feels because he might have been able to prevent the murder of his beloved Uncle Ben and realizes that with great power comes great responsibility.  Clark Kent?  What is his deal?  He becomes Superman because his parents were the salt of the Earth and so he of course becomes Superman.......wait, what?  That is pretty much the story of how Clark Kent becomes Superman in most of the stories in comics, movies, and tv.  There are some variations.  Sometimes he wears tights because they were made by his adoptive mom and sometimes his dad gave him the tights.  Until Smallville live action portrayals of Clark Kent becoming Superman have glossed over his transition.  Before Lois and Clark, the New Adventures of Superman, Clark Kent was the disguise and Superman the real person, which really doesn't make sense because if Clark decides to become Superman before he enters the larger world why not be John Smith?  Why create a Clark who can be traced back to Smallville and potentially lead enemies to his weakness and to his friends and family there?  Lois and Clark gave us a Clark that exists before Superman but it really doesn't give a good reason why dressing up in tights is a good idea and why he should waste his time fighting petty criminals instead of using his powers to end famine or global warming or the many other things he could do in order to save millions of lives.  The glossing over of this transition is most blatant in the historic Superman the Movie where teenage Clark (presumably having graduated from high school) leaves his elderly mother at the farm (after the neighbor has promised to give her a hand) and travels to the Arctic, creates the Fortress and then emerges 12 years later as Superman.  They give no explanation for anything that happened in that 12 year time period and again why does he head to the big city and establish himself as Clark Kent and not John Jones or Jack Sprat or anyone else?    Fortunately, we have Smallville.  Now Superman makes sense.  We know why he decides to become Superman.  We know why he is Clark Kent.  We know why he wears the tights and cape.  We know why he is with Lois Lane instead of spending every waking moment saving lives.  The ten year journey of teenage Clark Kent to the adult Boy Scout known as Superman makes it all come together.  I watched Smallville when it premiered and for the first three seasons.  After that I was too busy and I didn't have much time to watch tv so I missed the rest of Smallville and other shows (Buffy, Angel, Babylon 5, Farscape, etc.).  I did occasionally read comments about it, criticisms of some thing or another like the witches or Lexana but didn't pay much attention.  It was only after I had caught up on other shows I had missed (The Dead Zone, Crusade, etc.) that I remembered I had missed out on much of the last season of Lois and Clark from years ago which gave me some Superman nostalgia. I decided to watch those L&C episodes and then re-watch Superman the Movie and Superman II.  My nostalgia not sated and learning that Smallville was on its last season I thought, \\"Hey, I should watch Smallville.  It was an ok tv show.\\"  I got the first dvd and watched and said to myself, \\"I don't remember this show being this good.\\"  I then got the next disc and the next and hit the end of Season 2 and said to myself, \\"Wow, this is really good.\\"  I learned a valuable lesson, the way to watch Smallville is without commercials.  Sure, that sounds like a lesson for all shows except that Smallville is different.  It has the feel of a theatrical release and not a tv show.  As I watched more episodes I would cringe as I approached episodes that had been roundly criticized by \\"fans\\" of the show and I found that that criticism was not merited.  The Lana possessed by a witch storyline was just fine.  It wasn't bad at all.  It was actually good.  Lana getting involved with Lex?  It made sense.  Clark not telling Lana his secret even though lots of bad guys found out.  Again, made sense.  Clark not flying?  What a stupid complaint, seriously of all the complaints during the 10 seasons this was the dumbest one.  All of this taught me another lesson.  The best way to watch Smallville is with few breaks between episodes.  If a person watched the series regularly they had to endure commercial breaks,cliffhangers that lasted weeks or months and storylines that ran through numerous episodes or even seasons before being resolved along with references to past continuity.  It is no wonder that some people would complain about the actions of a character or something in an episode if they couldn't remember something that happened previously.  By not having a great deal of time pass between episodes the viewer will find Smallville a much more satisfying experience.  Even the actions of Jor-el make a lot more sense within a compressed viewing time frame.  So, to sum up, for the best viewing of Smallville watch at least one episode each day (two or three if it is a season ending cliffhanger).  A final comment.  Smallville is its own world.  It does not have to reflect any past Superman continuity.  In fact, Superman in comics has no real continuity.  He has a number of different origins as do his foes.  A fan of Superman should not be offended that Lois Lane meets Clark Kent long before he is Superman and she is a reporter.  In the Smallville world it works out great.  It isn't as if Lex has hair and Pete Ross is the alien from Krypton.  Smallville has added a number of great things to the Superman mythos that ought to be adopted to the official Superman story and probably will because they make his story so much better.  It will be a long time before we will ever have another comic book legend adapted to tv that is this great (prove me wrong del Toro!).

Fast forward to 2011, and it as if the past years never happened. We're in the exact same situation. If someone came on TV and the internet stirring up the current population, telling them all to shout \\"I'm mad as hell and I'm not going to take it any more\\" - they'd answer in volume. In fact our waves of protests seem to indicate that very thing is going on.   There are amazing actors in this movie, and they present a storyline that has timeless meaning. The anchorman is about to be kicked out for being too old, too out of date. When his inner rage erupts, he catches fire with the public who appreciates his honesty. His TV station vacillates between being nervous he'll hurt their reputation and thrilled as the ratings increase.   The ups and downs of his show pull other station members along with him. There's the woman who is all about high ratings at all cost. There's the old school news director who has concerns about morality.   The star of the show, though, is the television set. We hear how the \\"current population\\" barely reads a book or newspaper. They get all of their information fed to them, pre-chewed, from the TV set. Over the course of the movie we see how the masses can be mobilized - or pacified - all due to how the TV message is handed to them.  The only real difference is that many people now stare at the glowing internet screen rather than the glowing TV set on the wall.   A good movie to re-examine just how much things have stayed the same in the past few decades.

B004W2UBYW|5.0|Isaacson begins by telling readers that Jobs passion for perfection and simplicity, along with his ferocious drive, revolutionized six industries - PCs, animated movies, music, phones, tablet computing, and digital publishing, while making him the ultimate icon of inventiveness, imagination, and sustained innovation. Apple became the world's most valuable company in 2011, in stark contrast with its early record of numerous failed products that resulted in Jobs being forced out in 1985. Even Apple's early hits were marginal - eg. the Mac had only 128K memory, and no expansion slot. Worse, his post-Apple NeXT cost too much - $6,500, and soon died.) Jobs' formal education consisted of one semester at Reed College in Portland - costing his adoptive parents almost their entire life's savings, and 18 more months of 'dropping in' on classes as he saw fit. (The list included calligraphy, a learning experience he later labeled as 'invaluable.') Less formal education sources included a spiritual trip to India, primal scream therapy, and LSD. Like Hewlett and Packard, he teamed up with another genius, Steve Wozniak, and worked in his parents garage, combining their technical, business, and design skills.<br /><br />Isaacson's 'Steve Jobs' biography is based on 40 personal interviews over two years with Jobs after Jobs authorized the work in 2009 - he wanted his children to know him better; the author also interviewed more than a hundred adversaries, competitors, and friends. Readers get inside the house where Jobs grew up and displayed rebellion towards Paul (a high-school dropout who became a machinist) and Clara Jobs (a stay-at-home mom/bookkeeper), the adoptive middle-class parents who raised him, Jobs' rejection of Christianity after wondering why there were starving children, his first job (at Atari, involving the game Pong), meeting Wozniak through a neighbor and creating their first product - Blue Boxes that avoided long-distance phone charges, Apple's 1976 founding, his assessment of those brought in to run Apple after his 1985 ouster ('corrupt people who cared only about making money;' Jobs' outsourcing manufacturing to Asia, however, could be viewed similarly), marriage to former Goldman Sachs trader Laurene Powell, his turnaround of Apple after returning in 1997 (dumped board members, cut staff, killed loads of products, shifted manufacturing overseas, and took a $150 million lifeline from Microsoft in return for dropping a lawsuit and Microsoft's adapting its BASIC and Office software to run on Apple), how each Apple innovation first occurred to Jobs and/or his team, the rivalry with Bill Gates, Google (Android - he wanted it destroyed because it purportedly had stolen Apple's operating system, Google Docs), and others, his reinvention of the music industry via iTunes and the iPod, the motivations that drove Jobs, and his battle with pancreatic cancer.<br /><br />Apple I was built around a $20 MOS Technologies microprocessor that was equivalent to the $40 Motorola 6800, which in turn was cheaper than the Intel 8080. Jobs and Wozniak originally had a 10% partner who helped with legal documents and system documentation - however, that individual got cold feet about potential personal liability (the arrangement did not provide corporate liability protection) and cashed out for $2,300, vs. the $2.6 billion he'd be worth today. The original product lacked a power supply, case, and monitor, and cost $500 after being assembled in the garage of Jobs' parents. (Apple II added the power supply, case, and monitor capability.) Jobs pitched their product to Atari's CEO, who didn't appreciate Jobs' hygiene or bare feet and declined. Commodore decided to build its own computer instead of buying Apple in its entirety for several hundred thousand, per Jobs proposal. Needing $200K to go into production, Jobs was referred to Mike Markkula (passed over for chief marketing officer at Intel) who wrote most of Apple's initial business plan and turned Jobs on to going beyond the hobbyist market to also include people in regular homes and uses such as balancing one's checkbook and storing recipes. Markkula guaranteed a $250K line of credit for one-third equity, and insisted that Wozniack devote full-time to the project (was working full-time at H-P, and agreed to do so after first offering H-P the results of his work).  About 2,500 were sold in 1977, 210,000 in 1981.<br /><br />Former Apple CEO and Jobs boss John Scully describes Jobs as a minimalist - focus on the basics, make simplicity out of complexity. Jobs' 'heroes' included Einstein, Andy Grove, Bill Hewlett, Edwin Land, David Packard, Bob Noyce, Xerox's PARC research lab, and Sony. He registered 338 U.S. patents or patent applications for technology and electronic accessories. As for listening to customers, the bedrock of most firms, Apple/Jobs didn't bother, focusing instead on providing products and services they didn't even know they wanted, while sometimes omitting things they did want. Then there's Jobs vs. managerial theorists - his seeming involvement in every detail, temper tantrums, and unrealistic work expectations contradict all their theories about effective modern management style. Jobs instead saw his brutal putdowns as testing the strength of their thinking and convictions.<br /><br />Jobs saw Hewlett and Packard as having built a great company, subsequently being destroyed. To prevent this from happening to Apple, he worked to groom top talent, creating Apple University in 2008 that is intended to pass on Apple decision-making thinking to future top executives, thereby leaving a stronger legacy. Jobs recruited the dean of Yale's Business School, Joel Podolny, to run it; Podolny, in turn, recruited leading business professors to write Harvard Business School type cases as course material. As for Microsoft and Bill Gates - 'Bill is basically unimaginative and has never invented anything, which is why I think he's more comfortable now in philanthropy than technology; he just shamelessly ripped off other people's ideas.' (To be fair, Jobs acquired his own share of 'other people's technology,' starting with Xerox's mouse and GUI, and extending to 'multi-touch' that let people zoom in or out on content.)<br /><br />Unfortunately, Jobs' applied his 'think different' slogan for Apple to his personal cancer treatment as well. Acupuncture, veganism, herbal remedies, bowel cleansings, and spiritual healing took the place of what could have been life-saving surgery for nine important months - obviously ironic that someone who took his physical health far more seriously than most would make such a major error. (Less serious, though similarly off-base, Jobs prior belief that his diet prevented body odor.)<br /><br />Key Question: Was Jobs a visionary and inventor, or tweaker? Isaacson's biography suggests he was much more a tweaker. He borrowed the mouse and icons of the Macintosh from Xerox PARC, the first portable digital music players came out in 1996 - Apple's iPod came out in 2001 after Jobs realized the existing players 'truly sucked,' smart phones started coming out in the 1990s, while Apple's iPhone came out in 2007 - again after Jobs saw the existing cell phones all stand. The idea for the iPad came from an engineer at Microsoft who invited Jobs to his birthday party and badgered him (again) about how Microsoft's tablet PC software would eliminate all notebooks - Jobs, however, noted that the planned tablet used a stylus, and decided to show him would a tablet should be.<br /><br />Jobs' late-life advice to Larry Page of Google included cutting its number of products to avoid becoming like Microsoft, as well as avoiding becoming loaded down with B players. Jobs' rationale, despite anger at Google's entering the smart-phone market, was that 'the Valley had been good to him, and he wanted to return the favor.' Jobs told President Obama in the fall of 2010 that he was 'headed for a one-term presidency,' and suggested easing regulations and ending union education work rules. Years earlier advice to then President Clinton was for him to 'tell the country' if he'd been involved with Monica Lewinsky.<br /><br />Isaacson notes that success working with Jobs required expressing one's opinion, even if contrary to Jobs.' Otherwise he would lose any respect for that individual for lacking conviction.<br /><br />The 'good news' is that Isaacson's interviews with Jobs also portend an 'iTV' that would eliminate complex remotes for DVD players and cable channels, while also providing Web surfing with access to iOS. One more thing - Jobs viewed textbooks as another industry he wanted to transform.<br /><br />Bottom-Line: Isaacson's work is a worthy summary of a genius who lived every day to the fullest. Management/marketing researchers and general readers will enjoy and benefit from its reading.ilanthropy than technology; he just shamelessly ripped off other people's ideas.' ABBY  * You cannot seriously think it's okay to accept a diamond bracelet from a man you're dating, and then spend every night sleeping in another man's bed. There was nothing about her to make Travis go so crazy the moment he saw her; Abby was a placeholder for the female readers to pour all their fantasies into. The whole gambling plot came completely out of nowhere... and then faded into oblivion. It was pointless, and the mess of themes and plotlines marked it as a self-published book.  * The drama. Oh the melodrama. Completely over the top. Everything started and ended with screaming fits and somebody storming out. These characters were nowhere near mature enough for marriage and happily ever after, and - at nineteen or twenty - were far too old to be acting like idiotic kids.  * Why is every girl who isn't the perfect heroine a nasty slut? Why is any girl who partakes in casual sex disgusting, but the guys who do it are sexy?  THE WRITING  * A professional editor would have taken care of the atrocious pacing. As this is a self-published book, there was no professional editing. The book was FAR too long, dragging out the get together - fight - break up - make up - fight - break up - make up pattern, chapters and chapters longer than is should have gone on for.  * There were SO many typos, so many examples of poor grammar, and so many incorrect words and expressions. \\"A wide birth\\"? Seriously?! The author has trouble with the use of apostrophes, and got basic words wrong - YOUR and YOU'RE mix-ups stood out. The capitalisation of the endearment \\"baby\\" was very irritating.  Considering everything I found wrong with this book, the biggest issue for me is still Travis' endless violence. He was a criminal through and through.

Ten years ago I bought an early stud-finder tool at Home Depot which worked reasonably well, but it only indicated the general area of a stud, not the center. The Bosch tool improves on that.<br /><br />The unit has three function buttons: find wood, find metals, find live wires. It also has a power on button, a button to turn on the display back-light, and one to mute the speaker.<br /><br />PROS<br />Convenient hole in the unit for using a pencil to mark the location accurately<br />Indicates when it is nearing and when it reaches the center of the stud<br />More accurate determination of center of stud than my older stud finder<br /><br />CONS<br />The live wire and pipe finding did not work well for me<br /><br />TEST RESULTS<br /><br />WOOD: My old house has both lathe-and-plaster walls (wood slats run across all of the wall) and modern plaster walls. I was able to find stud centers accurately in both types of walls. This is a big improvement over my early stud-finder, which only indicated the range of where the stud might be, rather than its center. Since I put in some of the new walls, and have worked with the old ones, I do know where the studs are.<br /><br />METAL: I found that it gave false positives, perhaps because it was about 15 inches away from the gas meter, which is a lot of metal. It did accurately find one pipe I knew was there.<br /><br />LIVE WIRES: Moving the unit all around a power outlet, and a light switch outlet, it often failed to find the wire, but found it sometimes.  The manual says it is good up to two inches for this.  This is an area that could use improvement.<br /><br />BOTTOM LINE: It is a big  improvement over my existing stud finder for studs, and works very well for that. I take one star off for the problems with detecting metal and live wires, where it needs improvement.

0316017930|4.0|Outliers provides the reader with stories that are relatable: stories of opportunity, success, and failure.  When the individual's shared experiences stand-alone they are nothing more than the stories of people who have had opportunities and have succeeded or failed. What Gladwell does is go in depth in those stories to better determine why it is he or she succeeded or failed.   The readers are introduced to the town of Roseto whose citizens were \\"dying of old age. That's it\\" (Gladwell, 2008, p.3), the teams of hockey players who were better than everyone else because their players were born in certain months of the year, the stories of Bill Joy, Bill Gates, and the Beatles who put in 10,000 or more hours of practice in their respective fields and were on the receiving end of opportunities that were seemingly one in a million to achieve the greatness and success they have.   Each of these mentioned are outliers respective to the occurrences in their society and in their day and age.  The readers are also provided with stories of men named Chris Langan and Robert Oppenheimer.  Both gentlemen are extremely gifted in their thinking capabilities but differ in how the gentlemen react to the situations they came from, live in and how they handled and used opportunities that came their way to their advantage (One goes on to be a part of the Manhattan Project and the other is a drop out of college living on a rural farm).  What separates Chris from Robert as well as the stories of the other \\"outliers\\" is that, \\"outliers are those who have been given opportunities- and who have had the strength and presence of mind to seize them\\" (Gladwell, 2008, p. 267).    Part one focused on opportunities that were thought provoking and intriguing and highlighted ideas most readers are not exposed to. Part two however focused on legacy which was not necessary and distracted the awe factor of part one simply because it is seemingly common knowledge that culture influences and individual'ssuccess.  As educators Outliers was a tool for us in viewing students who come from all parts of the economic, cultural, and social spectrum and recognizing they all need opportunities to excel.

1400095204|5.0|This is an extraordinary book, which tells the story of the Igbo people, who seceded from Nigeria in 1967 in response to ethnic violence and formed the short-lived nation of Biafra.  Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie writes with spare but powerful prose, carrying the reader along with an account of everyday life amongst a set of  people from the professional class in Nigeria who suddenly, and to their own disbelief, find themselves in the midst of a horrifying civil war.   Adichie's main characters are richly portrayed:  the intense, politically motivated professor Odenigbo; his lover Olanna; her twin sister, the business woman Keinene;  Kaienene's lover Richard, a white man who becomes fiercely Biafran; and perhaps most of all, Ugwu, a village teen who is hired by Odenigbo as a \\"houseboy\\" and who eventually become a member of Odenigbo and Olanna's family. 0307477479|4.0|The recent recipient of a Pulitzer Prize, \\"A Visit from the Goon Squad,\\" is more a collection of interlinked short stories than a true novel, where each chapter  save, of course, the first) revolves around a character who's been introduced in an earlier one.  There's Sasha a thirty-something kleptomaniac; her boss Bennie, who we first meet as a divorced middle-aged record-producer still listening to the punk rock music of his youth; the younger Bennie, hanging out with a group of close friends whose band is named the Flaming Dildos; Lou, a music producer who becomes a lifelong mentor to Bernie after bedding his friend Jocelyn;  Rolph and Charlene, Lou's children, who accompany him on a safari to Africa where they witness a life-changing, terrifying event;  Bennie's wife Stephanie and her possibly psychopathic and definitely murdering brother Jules; and on and on.   Jennifer Egan's stories jump around in time and in place and in theme--and also in style.  She experiments with a range of styles, some traditional and some wildly experimental.  Her experiments sometimes fail:  for example,  her second-person story of a suicide, told by the victim himself, seemed to me quite forced.  Other times her post-modern experiments work brilliantly, most notably in her remarkably touching story-in-powerpoint--yes, powerpoint--narrated by a 12-year-old character, Allison Blake, about her life with an autistic brother.  There are other high points in the book, in chapters that have a more traditional style, including the one that tells the tale of the safari mentioned above.    In some ways this book recalls those of Salmon Rushdie or Zadie Smith, with their parade of seemingly dozens of quirky characters.  In others, it recalls the David Mitchell masterpiece \\"Cloud Atlas,\\"  which was similarly built on loose links between its chapters.  But where \\"Cloud Atlas\\" ultimately succeeds by building a coherent, compelling whole, \\"A Visit from the Goon Squad,\\" while fun to read--in  in places enormously fun--in the end disappoints in its failure to hang together and present the long arc of coherent character development and change that make a novel great.

0062011847|5.0|Sir Terry, a few years ago, was diagnosed as suffering from posterior cortical atrophy, a rare variant of Alzheimer's disease. It has affected his ability to type but not, I am very pleased to report, his ability to craft a fine novel. He dictates now, and uses an assistant to do the parts that can't be dictated. Physically, he may be impaired, but his mind, his sense of humor, his humanity and, importantly, his writing are as sharp as ever.<br /><br />Sam Vimes is a copper. When we first met him, in [[ASIN:0061020648 Guards! Guards!]], he was drunk and lying in the gutter of Ankh-Morpork. Over the course of subsequent novels, including [[ASIN:0061092193 Men at Arms]], [[ASIN:0061057649 Feet of Clay]], [[ASIN:B001SRRE6W Jingo [JINGO] [Mass Market Paperback]]], [[ASIN:0061020400 The Fifth Elephant]], [[ASIN:0060013125 Night Watch]] and subsquent novels, he has advanced himself considerably, married well, fathered a son and, at the apparent whim of his boss, the tyrant of Ankh-Morpork, he has been made a Duke. But he's still a copper, raised on the very mean streets of his city.<br /><br />Now he has to face the worst terror yet: a vacation in the country, at his wife's family estate. Of course, a copper can't escape crime by going out of town; a copper can't unpack his suitcase without a body turning up. But is it a murder when the victim is a goblin, regarded as mere vermin? Is it a crime? Vimes, as much as any character in Pratchett's vast body of work, reflects the values of Pratchett himself. Pratchett and Vimes regard the worst class of crime as treating a thinking creature as an object. Goblins are thinking, speaking, if smelly, beings. How can murdering or kidnapping them not be a crime?<br /><br />Sometimes the crime comes before the law.<br /><br />Pratchett skillfully weaves together numerous plot threads. From Lord Vetinari's initial pensive reaction to news from Pastor Oats to the strategies of Vimes' wife, Lady Sibyl, it's a remarkable piece of plotting. Prachett's trademark use of humor is as sharp as ever. Here is Vimes contemplating the law of trout streams:<br /><br />\\"He wondered how you could own a trout stream because, if that was your bit, it had already gurgled off downstream while you were watching it, yes? That means somebody else was now fishing in your water, the b****d! And the bit in front of you now had recently belonged to the bloke upstream; that bloated plutocrat of a fat neighbor probably considered you some kind of a poacher, that other b****d!\\"<br /><br />One more quotation, this from Vimes' first encounter with Miss Felicity Beedle, a very successful children's writer, and the favorite author of Vimes' son, young Sam:<br /><br />\\"Vimes, hand in hand with his son, walked toward the house of Miss Beedle thoughtfully, not knowing what to expect. He had little experience of the literary world, much preferring the literal one, and he had heard that writers spent all day in their dressing gowns drinking champagne.*<br /><br />* This is, of course, absolutely true.\\"<br /><br />It's what Pratchett does: ironic footnotes, wonderful characters, brilliant plots and deeply human stories, even if the stories often involve dwarfs, trolls, werewolves, vampires and, yes, goblins.<br /><br />It's true that Vimes wants to arrest the gods for getting it wrong (although he denies saying it); it's true that the Summoning Dark (see [[ASIN:0060815310 Thud!]]) still lies, caged inside of him; and it's true that for all his cunning he's not a particularly smart man. But he is a wonderful character, and a near-perfect means for Pratchett to make his points.<br /><br />Pratchett also has a gift for making points in front of the reader that don't sink in until much later. Such as the off-hand observation that Arachne, a filing clerk at the Anhk-Morpork embassy in Four Ecks, collects venomous spiders.<br /><br />Pratchett. writing about the fantasy genre, quoted Chesteron's defense of fantasy literature and children: \\"The objection to fairy stories is that they tell children there are dragons. But children have always known there are dragons. Fairy stories tell children that dragons can be killed.\\" Pratchett shows us that even the very worst dragons, including racism and blind arrogance, can be slain. For Pratchett, the weapon of choice is humor, and he wields it as skillfully as ever.<br /><br />This probably isn't the place to start reading the history of Sam Vimes. The links in this review might give you a better start. But Pratchett continues to astonish with his writing, his humor and his humanity. My very highest recommendation.ons. B005KCGIUY|4.0|Like everything Mr. Keenan does, it's hard to digest this all in one bite. Every album he and his various minstrels create evolves slowly to me. Rarely have I felt the same way about a Tool, A Perfect Circle, or Puscifer song as the first time it made its way into my head. Conditions of my Parole is no different.  It's a strange beast to be sure, and can be off-putting if you're not careful. The second you hit play (or drop the needle if you're old school) you're greeted by a beat that sounds like something Chris Brown would dance to. It's almost as if Maynard is daring you to stop listening. But you can't. You want to know what's behind door number two. The answer: a pretty good album.  The soundscape reflects immensely on their recording location of choice: Maynard's vineyard. You can almost picture them hammering out these songs in a cellar at two in the morning, sipping a Cabernet, then going to bed with smiles on their faces. Tracks like Green Valley, Telling Ghosts, and Tumbleweed could all pass for spaghetti western fodder (I hope you're listening Mr. Tarantino). The desert vibe is pretty palpable.  None the less, Conditions' architecture is a familiar one for Puscifer fans. Many of the band's themes are intact here. The album is solemn, savage, and stripped. Toma is your upbeat equivalent to Dozo. Man Overboard is the groovy answer for The Mission. And the title track serves as immature schtick in the same vein as Country B**er (It's a shame that song came to Maynard so early. It would have made a great home on this album). For me the standout is Monsoons, a sweeping epic that builds in the verses and thrills in the chorus when Devo Keenan contributes some beautiful cello play.  This album was a grower, not a shower. I wasn't sure at first if I would settle into the picture that Puscifer had painted. It's possible that you may not. But you'd be a fool to dismiss it as either boring or lazy. The depth is there, if you're willing to look for it.

This was the first consumer level IS lens canon offered, and the system is primitive by today's standards. I never owned the SACD version because i've always just had a DVD-Audio player but this release sounds good. I am bias since other than a  LFE channel it's not much different. Lastly you get a stereo mix LPCM @ 24/96khz which wouldbe on the SACD, otherwise this is going sound better than a CD simply because of 24 sample rate if not the inclusion of 96khz over 44khz cds have. honestly unless you are rocking thousands of dollar setup a up-sampling cd will sound about the same. I say up-sampled because it sounds a little more rich and vibrate compared to a normal cd even without the $$. B004EPZ07U|1.0|I avoided seeing \\"Green Lantern\\" when it hit the theaters, because the trailers looked bad and most reviews were harsh. But as the months went by, I became oddly curious to see this much-maligned film. Could it possibly be as bad as I'd heard...?  The answer to that question is, of course, yes - it really is *that* bad. So if, like me, you dodged this turkey initially but have a strange itch to see it now, I can confirm that this itch need not be scratched.  The film has so many problems that it's hard to know where to begin trashing it. First of all, despite the massive budget, it looks awful. The computer effects have an unconvincing, video-game feel to them, while the aliens are universally corny. Overall, the film has a hideous aesthetic sense - unless you happen to be fond of lime green, canary yellow, and fuchsia. The action scenes, meanwhile, have the hectic and totally unexciting quality of most contemporary action scenes. (How I long for the days when Hollywood knew how to properly thrill audiences with stunts, extras, and physical effects...)  Needless to say, the script is awful as well. The writers never give us any reason to like Hal Jordan, who is depicted as a self-involved creep (and played with zero depth by Ryan Reynolds). Another problem is that the relationship between Hal and Carol Ferris is poorly developed, as is their mutual connection to Hector Hammond. These three characters form the emotional core of the movie, yet their collective backstory is very sketchy indeed.  Also, I don't think the movie does a good job of \\"selling\\" the concept of Green Lantern. Alas, the idea of a superhero who creates green energy shapes out of pure willpower strikes me as a little too strange even by Hollywood standards. Plus, it raises all kinds of questions for me; if Green Lantern can really create *anything* with his ring, why does he settle for bland stuff like giant fists and (yawn) guns? And finally, all the chatter this movie serves up about the conflictbetween \\"willpower\\" and \\"fear\\" strikes me as abstract and silly. (Compare the baloney you get here to Batman Begins, which used the concept of \\"fear\\" in very intelligent ways to develop both the plot and the characters.)  In short, this thing stinks. Indeed, it's a textbook example of everything that's wrong with contemporary cinema. Compared to Green Lantern, the Thor movie is a masterpiece - while a real masterpiece like Amadeus exists in another universe entirely. So, I strongly advise you to avoid Green Lantern, just as you would avoid a poisonous yellow cloud of...erm...tangible fear, or something like that.

0312576463|2.0|At its core, \\"Freedom\\" is the story of a love triangle, involving three deeply stereotypical characters: 1) Richard Katz, a bad boy musician; 2) Walter Berglund, an environmentalist nice guy and general doormat of a human being; and 3) Patty Berglund, a shrill woman who used to play basketball, and now does nothing much. The star of this love triangle is Patty - and Franzen's great failure is that he never makes her even remotely interesting. Indeed, of the three main characters, only Walter truly evolves and comes alive; so, the segments of the book where he's the focus are pretty good, while the segments focusing on Patty get mired in her self-pity and general unpleasantness.  Of course, Franzen was too ambitious to write a book that's *only* about a love triangle. In an effort to make this thing more relevant, he loaded it up with timely cultural and political references. But while I tried very hard to figure out exactly what Franzen's political message could be, all I could detect was cynicism and anger. His repeated references to the Iraq War got me riled up, but didn't get me thinking about the war in a new way. It doesn't help that the major segment involving the war is deeply unconvincing; somehow, the Berglunds' 19-year-old son gets roped into a corrupt contract that involves selling defective Polish truck parts to the U.S. military in Iraq. Would a teenage kid really get involved with a deal like that? I think not, and even if Franzen based the segment on a true story, he totally failed to \\"sell\\" it properly.  Another big problem is Franzen's use of dialogue, which is often totally ridiculous. Almost every character speaks in the same cynical, ultra-intellectual voice, and uses words and phrases that no normal human being would use (e.g., \\"calendrically,\\" \\"obsessional,\\" and \\"Reaganite regressive\\"). His college-aged kids are especially unrealistic; do any teenagers you know use the word \\"confounding\\" or phrases like \\"don't be a tender daisy\\"? I didn't thinkso.   Franzen's prose is equally problematic: his writing style is too casual, too unambitious, and almost totally lacking in sensory detail. Also, it's sometimes tough to figure out whether you're listening to Franzen as narrator, or getting the third-person-limited viewpoints of individual characters. For example, there's a scene in which the Berglunds' son is reunited with his girlfriend after a long absence, and the prose describing his emotional reaction (an \\"all-organ melting\\") is totally absurd. Is the silly prose there because we're getting the son's perspective, or is the omniscient narrator at work (and not too clever himself)? Who knows. What I do know for certain is, if I ever included a phrase like \\"all-organ melting\\" in my own prose, I'd be laughed into oblivion.  In short, this book is pretty bad. And I really wanted to like it, because I agree with Franzen's views on contemporary American society (I think) and I'm sympathetic with this aims. I just can't tolerate stereotypical characters, bad prose, bad dialogue, and long unbelievable segments. I was left wondering how such a deeply flawed book could get such rave reviews; perhaps old media dinosaurs have nothing better to do these days but praise and support each other, even when it's undeserved? Anyway, I plan on never reading this again, and I will recycle my paperback copy at the earliest opportunity; Walter Berglund, at least, would approve of that.

B005HQ5S3G|5.0|Kelly Clarkson is my favorite contemporary pop singer, the best voice on the radio today, and in my opinion, very under-rated.  So, I have been looking forward to Stronger for several months, especially after her last release, All I Ever Wanted (an excellent CD) didn't appear to get the massive sales it so deserved.     So, now she's back - and Stronger is everything I want from Kelly, and more.  While it doesn't push new frontiers, what it does is take the best of what she has previously offered and adds a more mature, more confident tone to the music.  It is very much the offspring of both Breakaway and My December in parts, while in others, it shows growth beyond the boundaries of either of those two earlier albums.  Kelly loves to sing anthemic, guitar-driven music, she loves the sassy kiss-off songs, and she definitely can sell these song types better than anyone else.  If that is what you're looking to find on a KC album, then you will not be disappointed with Stronger, as several of these songs remain in that category.  But, on this album she has brought a soulfulness to the vocal delivery that hasn't been very obvious in the prior two releases, and her voice has never sounded better.  The production on these songs is top-notch, and Kelly's vocals are amazing.  If she isn't nominated for a Grammy for this album, it will be a crime.     So about the songs - unlike most albums, there is virtually no filler on this album, not even on the deluxe version.  In fact, it must have been very difficult to even choose which cuts to place on the standard version and which ones would be the bonus tracks.  Yes, they are all good, but even amongst the strength, there are some weaker moments.  Einstein has the cringe-worthy lyrical line \\"dumb plus dumb equals you\\" even while totally making me love the groove, and while I love the lyrics to You Can't Win, it is simply not as melodic as it should be.  The best songs, Mr. Know It All, What Doesn't Kill You (Stronger), Dark Side, The War Is Over, and You Love Me are among the best tracks she has ever recorded, and deserve to be hits!  The remaining songs, while not necessarily radio-friendly in today's Top-40 environment, still deserve a listen - Honestly, Standing in Front of You, and Hello are other stand-outs as well.     Bottom line - this is the kind of album that you can listen to and enjoy from beginning to end, no filler, no fluff, and held together by one of the most naturally talented vocalists of our time.  In an industry where Adele and Pink have moved beyond simply selling singles to the teenage listeners, to recording a cohesive body of work, and gaining an adult audience, Kelly deserves the same respect, and the same sales!      For those who purchased the deluxe version of Stronger, you are treated to four additional tracks...all excellent.  Don't You Wanna Stay, her hit country duet with Jason Aldean, Alone, and the very, very snarky \\"Don't Be A Girl About It' are all excellent.  The last track, The Sun Will Rise, features Kara Dioguardi, who is a talented vocalist, but really doesn't add much more to a good song than Kelly could have done herself.     Again - buy this album!  If you love pop-rock, and appreciate the quality, tone and delivery of Kelly's voice, you will not be sorry, and you just might become a big fan!

While it doesn't push new frontiers, what it does is take the best of what she has previously offered and adds a more mature, more confident tone to the music.  It is very much the offspring of both Breakaway and My December in parts, while in others, it shows growth beyond the boundaries of either of those two earlier albums.  Kelly loves to sing anthemic, guitar-driven music, she loves the sassy kiss-off songs, and she definitely can sell these song types better than anyone else.  If that is what you're looking to find on a KC album, then you will not be disappointed with Stronger, as several of these songs remain in that category.  But, on this album she has brought a soulfulness to the vocal delivery that hasn't been very obvious in the prior two releases, and her voice has never sounded better.  The production on these songs is top-notch, and Kelly's vocals are amazing.  If she isn't nominated for a Grammy for this album, it will be a crime.     So about the songs - unlike most albums, there is virtually no filler on this album, not even on the deluxe version.  In fact, it must have been very difficult to even choose which cuts to place on the standard version and which ones would be the bonus tracks.  Yes, they are all good, but even amongst the strength, there are some weaker moments.  Einstein has the cringe-worthy lyrical line \\"dumb plus dumb equals you\\" even while totally making me love the groove, and while I love the lyrics to You Can't Win, it is simply not as melodic as it should be.  The best songs, Mr. Know It All, What Doesn't Kill You (Stronger), Dark Side, The War Is Over, and You Love Me are among the best tracks she has ever recorded, and deserve to be hits!  The remaining songs, while not necessarily radio-friendly in today's Top-40 environment, still deserve a listen - Honestly, Standing in Front of You, and Hello are other stand-outs as well.     Bottom line - this is the kind of album that you can listen to and enjoy from beginning to end, no filler, no fluff, and held together by one of the most naturally talented vocalists of our time.  In an industry where Adele and Pink have moved beyond simply selling singles to the teenage listeners, to recording a cohesive body of work, and gaining an adult audience, Kelly deserves the same respect, and the same sales!      For those who purchased the deluxe version of Stronger, you are treated to four additional tracks...all excellent.  Don't You Wanna Stay, her hit country duet with Jason Aldean, Alone, and the very, very snarky \\"Don't Be A Girl About It' are all excellent.  The last track, The Sun Will Rise, features Kara Dioguardi, who is a talented vocalist, but really doesn't add much more to a good song than Kelly could have done herself.     Again - buy this album.  If you love pop-rock, and appreciate the quality, tone and delivery of Kelly's voice, you will not be sorry, and you just might become a big fan!

0345522478|3.0|2.75 Stars.  Okay, the good things first.  The writing is solid in Hounded and the pacing is good.  The characters are interesting.  There is not any chapter that drags or is slow.  This is a unique thing in this genre, particularly for a first book!  I went into this book with high expectations and I have learned that is usually not going to be a good thing for the book I am reading, with a few exceptions.  This review concentrates on what disappointed me about this book.  It seems that lately, with a few exceptions, it is hard for me to read an urban fantasy book and not find problems with the plot or storyline.  I did enjoy the book somewhat, so ......  This book reads to me like \\"urban fantasy lite\\".  The character development is not overly deep, the relationships are not thoroughly flushed out, and the plot is pretty simplistic:  Bad guy is chasing hero for centuries.  Bad guy wants to defeat hero and take possession of powerful item that hero possesses.  Hero has a lot of power, he can use it almost indefinitely and he is funny.  Oh, and most women who see hero WANT him.  Hey, I probably would want Atticus too by the way he was described.  Hearne tells the readers rather than shows them the world building and the history of the characters.  And he utilizes Atticus's conversations with people he meets as a method to do this.  I just find this as a weak plot device to teach the readers and further the plot.  Plus it just isn't as interesting.  My favorite urban fantasy books have characters who have access to power or have supernatural traits, but they can only wield their power with a heavy cost.  That makes sense to me and the cost seems to balance things.  Books such as Mercy Thompson by Briggs and the Dresden Files by Jim Butcher are great examples of this.  Power is not easy to come by.  And those that carry such power, not only have to make sacrifices but they also wear heavy battle scars - emotionally and physically.  Well, there are no such costs or scars in this book.  Atticus (the hero) appears to have access to an unending source of power and quite a bit of supernatural support - but at what cost?  None, so it appears.  Sleeping on the grass?  Breathing fresh air?  Those things are not a true cost.  I never doubted that Atticus would win/succeed/make it away fast.  I enjoy wondering in my books if everything will work out and I like it when authors surprise me sometimes and not all works out.  Suspense and tension are a positive for me in books I read.  For me, this book had neither.    Atticus has been alive for 21 centuries, he references having a son yet he is light hearted and does not appear to be affected by the passage of time or the loss of those he loved.  He acts like a man from this time period, he acts like maybe not a 21 year old, but a 28 year old.  He loves watching sports on Sunday?  He loves Kevin Costner movies?  I just found this not to be believable.  I also thought it was a weak plot device to have Atticus surprised and manipulated so easily.  For example, by Flittish during the opening hunt scene or by the coven of witches.  Why does he give Malina so much information? Why does he give the bar maid so much information during their first full conversation?  This openness and lack of vetting people wasn't consistent with a guy who had been alive for 21 centuries.  I saw that they were not trustworthy early on and I am much younger than Atticus.  I understand why Kevin Hearne used these characters in a way to titillate and then surprise the readers, but couldn't have it been done in a more convincing way?  Oddly enough (perhaps this says more about me than the book), I just did not think the book was too funny.  It might just be me, but I don't think it is funny to get revenge on a neighbor by sending your dog over to poop on his lawn, repeatedly.  Yes, I know the neighbor was mean and reported him to the police.  But hey, in the neighbor's defense usually the stuff reported was dangerous andillegal.  And then to talk about the revenge tactic, repeatedly, just not funny for me.  Nor do I think it is funny to read dialogue that includes making fun of the way somebody looks.  I understand that the witch really didn't look that beautiful and was wearing fake skin, but why go there?  But I may just be uptight.  Another example of mean humor that I just did not enjoy, was the giving of wedgies to the paramedic.  Why was Atticus doing this?  Why is this funny?  The entire discussion of suing the police was ridiculous.  Atticus knows that he has committed crimes by modern day standards.  The police are rightly investigating Atticus.  And then Atticus learns that the police and a specific officer are under the control of his enemy.  Why would he sue them?  Just for revenge and money?  When in reality, the policy either had no control over their actions and/or were acting on legitimate tips!  This entire discussion was really distasteful for me, yes sue when you have been wronged but this wasn't one of those situations.    What I liked about the book.  The pacing is fast, it is refreshing to read an urban fantasy from a male point of view, the cast of characters - despite being one dimensional, a few were interesting and I want to know more about them.  I plan on moving on to Book 2, because, well I bought it, I signed up for a buddy read and I am slightly intrigued as to whether the series will get stronger.  Many urban fantasy books start out with a great premise, like this book, and need a few books to get really strong.  So because it was not a bad book and because I somewhat enjoyed it, I will read it again.    I recommend this book to people who are interested in a light read.

0446547654|5.0|\\"The Best of me\\" by Nicholas Sparks follows the love story of Dawson Cole--a young man from a never do well family, who is sent to prison after he accidentally runs over the town's doctor, and upon release is nearly killed during an explosion in his new oil rig job--and Amanda Collier, his high school sweetheart who came from a wealthy family, and who, all those years later, is a married stay-at-home mom who occasionally ventures out to volunteer at the town's medical center. As Dawson and Amanda visit Tuck's estate and fulfill his wishes, they fall in love with each other all over again. Elsewhere in town, his cousins Ted and Abee found out his back in town and want to plot their revenge for Dawson beating them up all these years ago. Not satisfied with a confrontation, Ted plots to murder Dawson. Meanwhile, Abee is caught up in a romance of his own--one that ultimately turns dangerous for all the participants involved.  My thoughts: as in many of Sparks' previous novels, this story is told from different points of view. This is an effective device that Sparks uses, letting the reader get intimately acquainted with all of the characters. Right off the bat, we know that Dawson wears his heart on his sleeve--and is a vulnerable character--weighted down by guilt, lost love, and loneliness. Amanda is less of a dreamer than Dawson--preferring to stay in the present, and deal with the cards she's been dealt. Ted and Abee are both emotionally unstable, violent characters who lack any sort of morality or practicality. And Tuck is a father-figure character, who is above any of thenegative events taking place throughout the book, and more of a symbol of how the characters should have acted, but never did.  There's some paranormal elements to this story, but also a kind of nostalgic feel of lost time. You really root for Dawson and Amanda to get back together. But Sparks makes their relationship a mystery--up until the last third of the book. The conflicts created by the presence of Ted and Abee, Amanda's unpleasant mother, and Dawson's own demons are nicely incorporated into the plot. The ending is unpredictable, as the storylines of different characters are unexpectedly connected. The character who seemed so minor only a few pages ago, now has a major role in the story's outcome. And the story keeps you guessing until the last chapters.

The only song that I'm really not impressed with is \\"Sucker for a Superstar\\" which sounds like something leftover from Aquarius.  It's a real shame that there's no North American release date in sight for this album.  It is light years better than anything they've done before.  So many of the songs would do so well on the dance charts.  If you're an Aqua fan, this should be a no brainer...even with the bloated import price.

These are some humorous, annoying, even likable guys scraping to make a living through any client and situation possible. They're propped up a the tough secretary. They're bottom feeders. Along comes David Zinc, who can no longer stomach the hundred-hour work weeks at a legal firm where 600 other lawyers are employed. He goes off the rails, decides to check out one fine morning, and ends up drunk hours later on the steps at the ignoble Finley & Figg. Grisham gives us some great characters, three-dimensional, likable, understandable, despicable, and everything in between. This is the end of the first Era of Metallica, what I call the Hungry phase, but also can be called the Cliff phase, because this is the last album that Cliff Burton was on before he died. A truly tragic loss. I've long suspected that James was more Metal than either Lars or Kirk, and it's my opinion that Cliff was more like James, than Lars or Kirk, and I believe this album compared to future albums showed that Cliff balanced out the team.   Which is the best? Ride the Lightning, or Master of puppets? I answer, neither. Ride the Lightning is more Melodic awesomeness, and Master of Puppets are more soul crushing thrash. Both Albums are among the greatest heavy metal albums, simple as that.   *Battery* like Ride the Lightning Album, the opening song begins with a slow intro, but this one sounds a little less serious, there is no false lull into a sense of peace here, no, this song is more like you better be ready to have your skull crushed under the heal of our greatness. This is my favorite Thrash Type Metallica song, and it's one of their fastest ever, and certain live versions are even faster than the studio one. Great beat of metal mayhem as it rocks your soul.  \\"Master of Puppets* By this point your enjoying yourself listening to this album, especially if this is a first time (where have you been!) and suddenly this song hits, and wow what a song. The epic power of the repetition of \\"Master, Master, Master of puppets pulling the strings...\\" is just great. It take melodic slow down with a Kirk Solo, and then hammers you with a riff that builds you up to the epic final part of the song. Like Ktulu it's based on a H.P. Lovercraft story, and personally it reminds me of a good horror movie, of the monster variety. Much like the album concentrates less on Solos, and more on Riffs so this song actually seems to me to come off as more Thrash than \\"Ride the Lightning,\\" even though this song is actually slower than most of that album. Great Song.  *Welcome Home (Sanitarium)* It seems to be Metallica will always find an epic slower song to play on its albums and MoP is no exception to that rule. This is the most melodic song of the album. This song much like the song One on the next album starts up slow and deals being trapped, difference here is that you are trapped in your insanity, or in a mental war, as opposed to One where you are trapped in your own body. Overall, this a great song which continues to rise in force from slow and melodic to their strong fast Thrash sound, yet it still maintains its melodic nature even in the end. One of their greatest.   *Disposable Heroes* As a former army soldier myself, I can identify with this song a personal level, as many of the lyrics I tend to picture an uncaring officer barking orders to me and others around me. Much like the song, which shows the hammer of power over you, the soldier, this song hammers at you with all fastness of Thrash, it maintains its singular speed almost from front to back, and is one of the best songs on the album, but surprisingly less notable. I still prefer Ktulu from \\"Lightning\\" but still. This song is notable for among other things having base guitar solos... which is one echoing reason as to why some people correctly believe that Cliff Burton was the greatest Bass Guitar player to ever live. I would call this one of the most unusual songs in the Metallica Library. Trippy, yet awesome.   *Damage Inc* I don't have the necessary tech to verify this but I honestly believe that this may be the fastest Studio Metallica song. I looked it up, and according to forums, this is the second fastest song actually, after Dyer's Eve. Closer inspection does seem to suggest that Damage Inc is the second fastest song by Metallica. Overall, I love the opening of incredible as the song tells you that you are about to hear something truly great. Then comes the smashing of the drums in concert with the guitars, and you have Damage Inc, one of the fastest, greatest Metallica songs ever.   Overall, this Metallica at the height of their Thrash sound, now that is not to say Justice doesn't have it's share of Thrash, and even as I said above, Dyer's Eve is actually their fastest song, but after Master of Puppets, Metallica would never be quite so Thrash sounding again. This truly is one of the greatest heavy metal albums of all time and the example of why I love metal. A great album to buy. Rating? Is there any doubt? 5 for 5 Stars!

B003Y5H5JM|4.0|After watching Julie Taymor blathering on about The Tempest, it's difficult to understand why this movie is as good as it is.
